Refutation of the Claim that a Convert Never Changes Belief
Baqillani refutes the argument that a convert never abandons his original belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses internal experience and sensory evidence to refute the claim that religious converts never truly change their beliefs.

Core Idea
Denying religious conversion contradicts what we know from ourselves.

Baqillani encounters a skeptical argument that a person who converts from one religion to another is not the same person who held the original belief — so they never really changed. He dismisses this as absurd, pointing to our own internal experience of changing beliefs and our ability to criticize our past beliefs. This lesson examines his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's argument
Read the opponent's claim: 'The one who becomes a zindiq and a Jew is not the one who was a Magian.' Notice that they are trying to separate the person's identity from their beliefs.
This is a common skeptical move.
2
See Baqillani's logical consequence
Baqillani points out that if the opponent were correct, then no one would ever change a belief they held as true. Think about why this is a problem.
3
Notice Baqillani's appeal to experience
Baqillani argues that we find in ourselves beliefs that are different from what we previously held, and we even criticize our old beliefs after changing. This is a direct appeal to self-awareness and sense perception.
He calls the opponent's denial 'juhud al-darura wa al-hiss' (denial of necessity and sense).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : الذي تزندق وتهوّد غير الذي كان مجوسياً، قيل لهم : فعلى هذا ما انتقل أحد قط عن حق اعتقده ؛ والمعتقد للمجوسية على ما هو عليه، وإن صار إلى التدين باليهودية والزندقة، لم يفارق ما اعتقده قط ولا برىء منه وهذا جحد الضرورة والحس لأن الإنسان يجد من نفسه اعتقاداً لغير ما كان معتقداً له وذَمَّه لما كان عليه بعد مدحه له ؛ فلا معنى لمناظرة من انتهى إلى هذا الحد
They said: The one who becomes a zindiq and a Jew is not the one who was a Magian. It was said to them: According to this, no one has ever transferred from a belief he held as true. And the one who held the Magian belief as it is, even if he adopts Judaism and Zindiqism, he never departs from his belief nor becomes free of it. This is a denial of necessity and sense perception, because a person finds in himself a belief for something other than what he used to believe and criticises what he was upon after having praised it. So there is no meaning in debating someone who has reached this level.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument that a person who changes religion never really abandons his original belief. The opponent claims that the individual who becomes a zindiq (heretic) and a Jew is a different person from the one who was a Magian. Baqillani responds that if this were true, then no one would ever leave a true belief, which contradicts experience. He explains that a person finds himself holding a belief different from his previous one and even criticizes his former belief after having praised it. This is a clear denial of what is necessary and of sense perception. Therefore, there is no point in arguing with someone who has reached such an extreme position.

Key Terms
زندقة
تهوّد
مجوسية
جحد الضرورة والحس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Baqillani is denying the possibility of religious conversion altogether.
Correction: Actually, Baqillani is defending the reality of conversion against a skeptical argument that denies it.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what does the opponent's claim entail?
A) That everyone remains in their original religion
B) That no one ever changes belief
C) That all conversions are insincere
D) That sense perception is invalid

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the claim that converts never change belief by pointing to our own internal experience of changing beliefs and criticizing past convictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever experienced a genuine shift in a deeply held belief? Can you recall the moment you saw your former self with criticism after having praised it? If not, could it be that you have never truly examined your faith?
?
The passage says that arguing with someone who denies the reality of belief change is pointless. Are there areas in your life where you deny obvious realities about yourself—like your own capacity for change—because it's uncomfortable?
?
You claim to believe in Islam. But is this belief a lived reality that you have found in yourself, distinct from what you were raised with, or are you simply repeating what you were upon? This lesson demands that you ask: have I chosen my faith with conviction, or am I a passive follower?

Mirroring Arguments as a Dialectical Refutation
Baqillani records and refutes an objection about God's knowledge and sending.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the technique of mirroring an opponent's argument to refute it, leaving them without an answer.

Core Idea
Mirroring an opponent's reasoning can be a decisive refutation in kalam.

This lesson examines a short but characteristic example of dialectical argumentation in kalam. Baqillani presents an objection from opponents who interpret certain names and knowledge in a specific way. His response is not to dismantle their argument piece by piece, but to turn it back on them, showing that a parallel counter-argument leaves them speechless. This technique is common in theological debates and reveals the importance of logical symmetry.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the brevity and the two-part structure: 'They said... It was said to them...'
The passage is a fragment; context is missing, but the structure is clear.
2
Identify the opponent's objection
The opponents claim that a certain name is with Allah before creation and in His knowledge, linking it to sending and empowerment. They offer 'other such interpretations' (تأويلات).
The exact subject is unclear, but the form of an interpretative argument is recognizable.
3
Identify Baqillani's refutation
Baqillani replies by stating that a similar argument was given to them (مثله فيما احتجوا به) and they have no answer for it. This is a mirroring technique: using the opponent's own style of reasoning against them.
The phrase 'ولا جواب عنه' emphasizes the conclusiveness of the refutation.
4
Reflect on the dialectical strategy
Consider why mirroring is effective. It avoids the need to refute each detail; instead, it shows that the opponent's method leads to an unacceptable consequence or a stalemate.
This strategy is common in later kalam works, especially in refutations of skepticism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : أراد أن إسمي عند الله قبل خلق الدنيا وفي علمه، وعنده حيث مد الأرض والعلم بإرسالي وتمليكي أو غير ذلك من التأويلات قيل لهم مثله فيما احتجوا به ولا جواب عنه .
They said: He intended that my name is with Allah before the creation of the world and in His knowledge, and with Him where He spread the earth and knowledge (العلم) with my sending and my empowerment or other such interpretations. It was said to them the like of what they argued with, and there is no answer for it.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a brief objection-and-refutation exchange. The opponents put forward an argument concerning a name that is with Allah before creation, and His spreading the earth and knowledge, linking it to the claim of being sent and empowered. They offer other similar interpretations. The author then states that a parallel counter-argument was given to them, and they have no answer for it. The passage is condensed and lacks broader context, making the precise subject unclear, but the structure shows a typical dialectical response: mirroring the opponent's reasoning against them.

Key Terms
تأويل
احتج
جواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this passage presents a fully developed argument that can be understood in isolation.
Correction: The passage is a fragment from a larger debate; the exact subject of the objection and the details of the mirroring argument are not given, so its full meaning requires surrounding context.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani say about the opponents' argument?
A) It is completely invalid.
B) They were given a similar counter-argument and have no answer.
C) It proves the need for prophets.
D) It is based on rational proofs.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes an objection not by detailed analysis, but by mirroring the opponent's own reasoning, a dialectical tactic that leaves them without an answer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When the opponents offered 'other such interpretations' (تأويلات), think of a time you used interpretation to avoid a clear truth from Allah. How did that feel? Now, seeing Baqillani's response—simply turning their argument back—are you willing to let Allah's truth stand without twisting it to fit your desires? What will you do today to surrender to a difficult truth in your life?
?
The mirroring technique shows that your own reasoning can be used against you. Is there an argument you hold onto that, if mirrored, would collapse? Examine your heart: are you defending a position out of pride or truth? Commit to finding the answer this week.
?
Baqillani's refutation ends with 'no answer for it' (ولا جواب عنه). When have you felt that silence before Allah's truth—perhaps confronted with your own sin or a clear command you ignored? Will you remain silent and accept, or will you keep arguing? What is one step you can take today to stop arguing and start obeying?

Refuting the Indistinguishability of Ritual Acts and the Builder Analogy
Baqillani responds to two objections regarding the distinctiveness of ritual acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Baqillani refutes objections that claim ritual acts are no different from ordinary actions using both direct reasoning and an analogy from a builder.

Core Idea
God's command makes acts worshipful, not inherent qualities.

Imam al-Baqillani addresses critics who argue that there is no intrinsic difference between ritual acts like sa'i or circumambulation and other actions. He first concedes that God could have commanded other acts, then refutes the builder analogy by showing that change is permissible if it benefits the created being. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the two objections raised by the critics.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the first objection and response
Find where the critics say there is no difference between Safa-Marwa and other places. Then see Baqillani's response: if God willed, He could command any place.
3
Identify the builder analogy and response
The critics compare God to a wise builder who would not destroy his work. Baqillani responds by introducing the principle of benefit for the created being.
4
Understand the principle of benefit
Baqillani argues that if an action benefits the accountable person, then commanding it is good, even if it involves changes or destruction.
This refutes the objection that destruction is always bad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولكم إنه لا فرق بين الصفا والمَرْوَة والسعي بينهما وبين غيرهما يتعبد ولا بين البيت الحرام وبين غيره فهو كما وصفتم ولو شاء الله أن بالسعي في كل بقعة والتوجه إلى كل جهة لساغ ذلك منه إذا عرض به لثوابه ولم يكن ذلك ناقضاً لحكمته. ويقال لهم : وكذلك لم تجدوا حكيماً بنى أحسن البنيان وصوّر أكمل الصور وأشرفها ثم نقضها وهدم صورها وقبحها وذهب ببهجتها وشوه خلقها ؛ فإن قالوا : إذا كان في ذلك مصلحة المخلوق جاز تغيير خلقه وقلب صفته ومحو محاسنه، قيل لهم : وكذلك إذا كان صوم النهار وقيام الليل وتقبيل الحجر والطواف والسعي ورمي الجمار يعود بصلاح المكلف حسن تكليفه وكان ذلك أحسن في العقل إن كان فيه حسن من إتلاف نفس المكلف وإبطال حياته وهدم صورته ومحو محاسنه وإبطال عقله وحواسه ولا جواب لهم عن ذلك.
And as for your saying that there is no difference between Safa and Marwa and the sa'i between them and other acts of worship, nor between the Sacred House and other places, it is as you described. And if God had willed that sa'i should be performed in every place and prayer facing every direction, that would be permissible from Him if He made it a means to reward, and that would not contradict His wisdom. And it is said to them: Likewise, have you not found a wise builder who built the best structure and formed the most perfect and noble images, then demolished them, destroyed their forms, made them ugly, removed their beauty, and disfigured their creation? If they say: If there is benefit for the created being in that, then changing its creation, altering its attribute, and erasing its beauties is permissible. It is said to them: Likewise, if fasting during the day, standing at night, kissing the Black Stone, circumambulation, sa'i, and throwing the stones bring benefit to the accountable person, then imposing that as a duty is good, and that is more excellent in reason—if there is excellence in it—than destroying the accountable person's soul, annihilating his life, destroying his form, erasing his beauties, and destroying his intellect and senses. And they have no answer to that.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. The objectors claim there is no intrinsic difference between the ritual acts (sa'i between Safa and Marwa, circumambulation of the Kaaba) and any other similar actions. Baqillani responds that if God had commanded those other actions as worship, they would be permissible and not contradict divine wisdom. He then addresses a second objection using an analogy: the objectors argue that a wise builder would not destroy his beautiful creation. To this, Baqillani replies that if there is a benefit for the created being, change is permissible. He then applies this principle to acts like fasting and the pilgrimage rituals: if these duties benefit the morally accountable person, then commanding them is good, and it is better in reason than destroying that person entirely. The argument concludes that the objectors have no valid response.

Key Terms
الصفا والمروة
السعي
البيت الحرام
المكلف
تكليف
صوم النهار
قيام الليل
تقبيل الحجر
الطواف
رمي الجمار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the value of acts is inherent in the actions themselves.
Correction: Baqillani emphasizes that it is God's command that determines merit.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Baqillani's response to the builder analogy?
A) A wise builder never destroys his work.
B) Change is permissible if it benefits the created being.
C) The objectors have a valid argument.
D) God's wisdom is beyond human understanding.

Key Takeaway
Ritual acts are distinguished solely by divine command, and any change in creation is permissible if it benefits the created being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you perform rituals like prayer, fasting, and Hajj primarily because they are commanded, or do you rely on feeling their benefit? If God's command alone is sufficient, are you willing to obey even when you see no personal advantage?
?
The passage argues that commanding worship is more excellent than destroying your senses. Are you treating your acts of worship as life-giving commands from your Creator, or as empty routines? What would change if you truly believed that each command is a mercy designed for your benefit?
?
If you were faced with a command that seems unreasonable or pointless to you, would you submit like Baqillani teaches, or would you object using your own reasoning? Can you identify an area in your life where you have resisted a divine command because it didn't 'make sense'? Will you now submit fully?

Silence as Endorsement: Objection and Two Responses
Baqillani refutes the objection that silence does not always imply endorsement of a report.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the objection that silence about a report does not necessarily imply endorsement, using the distinction between manifest doctrines and witnessed events.

Core Idea
Silence is contextual; customary practice determines whether silence implies consent.

In this passage, Baqillani confronts a subtle objection: if opponents can remain silent about a false doctrine without approving it, then why should the Companions' silence about miracles imply endorsement? He offers two distinct arguments that rest on the different nature of claims about witnessed events versus opinions. Let's unpack his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the questioner's analogy between silence on false doctrines and silence on reports of miracles.
The objector tries to undermine the argument from silence by showing that silence can occur without approval.
2
Identify the first response
Baqillani appeals to the view of many Muslims that silence about a manifest false doctrine is impossible without denial, so such silence indicates consensus.
This response refutes the analogy by denying that silence about manifest falsehoods is permissible.
3
Understand the second response
He argues that custom differs: silence about opinion-based doctrines (which may be rare or under deliberation) is not the same as silence about claims of witnessed events, where silence indicates truth.
The key distinction is the type of claim: an opinion versus a reported event.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how these arguments support the reliability of tawatur (mass transmission) and the argument from silence in historical reports.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : أليس قد يجوز عندكم إمساك المخالفين عن القول والمذهب الظاهر فيهم مع خلافهم عليه واعتقادهم لفساده؟ فإن لم يدل إمساكهم على توثيقهم له واعتقادهم إياه فما أنكرتم أيضاً من مثل ذلك في الإمساك عما يدَّعي على الجماعات الكثيرة حضوره ومشاهدته إذا أمسكوا عن إنكار ذلك في أنه غير دال على توثيقهم للخبر واعتقاد صحته؟ قيل له : لا يجب ما قلته من وجوه . أحدها أن كثيراً من المسلمين يُحيل ظهور المذهب التي تعتقد فساده وخطأ الدائن به والذاهب إليه من غير إنكار منها لـه وردها على قائله ويجعل السكوت على القول الظاهر فيها إجماعاً على تصويبه وبمنزلة النطق بتصديقه وتصحيحه ولا يُفرّقون بين أن يكون ذلك القول بين العلماء والأئمة مع السكت من فروع الدين أو من أصوله التي يقع في مثلها التأثيم والتفسيق فهذا الاعتراض زائل عن هؤلاء . والجواب الآخر أن العادة في ذلك مختلفة؛ وللسكوت على المذهب المستخرج بالقياس والدليل علل تقتضي السكت عنه ليست في القول المدَّعَى على الناس حضوره ومشاهدته . والأصل في ذلك أن العادة لم تجر بإمساك الجماعات عن إنكار كذب كما لم تجر بنقل الجماعة الكذب وكتمان ما شوهد وسمع لما بيناه من قبل ولما جعلهم الله عليه من تفرق الدواعي والأغراض وليس كذلك العادة في المذهب المقول من ناحية الرأي والقياس لأنه قد يكون المعتقد لصحته عدداً قليلاً يجوز إمساك مثلهم عن إظهار مذهبهم وقد يكون الأكثر منهم عدداً في مُهْلة النظر والروية وممن لم
If a questioner says: 'Is it not possible in your view for opponents to remain silent about a statement and a manifest doctrine among them while they are in opposition to it and believe in its corruption? And if their silence does not indicate their trust in it and their belief in it, then why do you not also permit the same regarding silence about what is claimed to have been witnessed by large groups—their silence about denying that does not indicate their trust in the report and belief in its correctness?' It is said to him: 'What you have said is not necessary, for several reasons.' One of them is that many Muslims consider it impossible for a doctrine that they believe to be corrupt and erroneous for its adherent and follower to become manifest among them without any denial of it or rejection of its proponent, and they consider silence about a manifest statement among them to be consensus upon its correctness and in the position of verbal expression affirming and correcting it. And they do not differentiate between that statement being, with silence, among scholars and the imams from the branches of religion or its principles in which tathim and tafsiq occur. So this objection is removed regarding these people. The other answer is that custom differs in this matter; silence about a doctrine derived by analogy and evidence has causes that necessitate silence about it, which are not present in the statement claimed to have been witnessed by people. The basis of this is that custom does not proceed with groups remaining silent about denying falsehood just as it does not proceed with groups transmitting falsehood and concealing what was witnessed and heard, as we have explained before, and because Allah has placed them upon divergence of motivations and desires. Custom is not like that regarding the doctrine stated from the standpoint of opinion and analogy, because it may be that the number of those who believe in its correctness is small, and the silence of such people about manifesting their doctrine is permissible, and it may be that the majority of them are in the time of consideration and deliberation, and among those who have not...
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector argues that silence does not always imply endorsement, using the example of Muslims remaining silent about false doctrines. If silence about false doctrines is possible without approval, then the Companions' silence about miracles could also be without approval. Baqillani gives two answers. The first: Many Muslims consider that silence about a manifest false doctrine is impossible without denial; they treat such silence as tacit consensus (ijmā‘). So the objection does not apply to those who hold this view. The second answer: Custom regarding silence about doctrines derived by opinion and analogy is different from custom regarding silence about claims of witnessed events. People may remain silent about a rare opinion because the number of its believers is small or because they are still deliberating. However, about witnessed events, it is not customary for a large group to remain silent about a lie; their silence indicates truth. Thus, the analogy fails.

Key Terms
إمساك
المخالفين
السكوت
الإجماع
عادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Silence is always ambiguous and never implies consent.
Correction: Baqillani shows that silence can indicate consent when the claim is about a manifest public event, because custom prevents large groups from staying silent about falsehoods.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason Baqillani gives to reject the objection that silence does not imply endorsement?
A) Many Muslims consider silence about a manifest false doctrine impossible without denial, treating it as consensus.
B) The Companions were divinely protected from error.
C) The objection is not valid because the questioner is an unbeliever.
D) Silence always implies consent in Islamic law.

Key Takeaway
Silence about a witnessed event, unlike silence about a debated opinion, implies endorsement because custom prevents a large group from remaining silent about a false common experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a situation in your life where you remained silent about something you knew to be false. In light of this lesson, did your silence imply consent? What would it mean for you to speak the truth now? Commit to one specific action this week to break your silence.
?
When you hear a claim about an event that many people say they witnessed, do you trust it solely because of the crowd? How does Baqillani's distinction between opinions and witnessed events change the way you evaluate the reports you hear daily? Write down one report you accepted recently and check whether it meets the criteria of this lesson.
?
The objection in the passage assumes that silence about a false doctrine is possible without reproach. Are there false doctrines in your community that you silently tolerate? What would it cost you to speak out, and what would it cost you to remain silent? Decide now: what is the first step you will take to align your silence with truth?

Refutation of the Claim that Knowledge of Transmission Must Be Necessary
Baqillani refutes the objection that if transmission were true, knowledge would be necessary, showing it leads to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that requiring necessary knowledge from transmission would destroy the reliability of all reports.

Core Idea
If knowledge must be necessary, no report can be trusted.

The opponent argues that if the Muslim transmission of miracles were true, everyone would be compelled to believe it. Since not everyone believes, the transmission must be false. Baqillani responds by showing the devastating consequences of this reasoning: it would allow us to accuse entire communities of lying, ultimately nullifying all historical knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening statement of the opponent: 'If it were as they claim, we would have known their truthfulness necessarily.' Grasp why they think necessary knowledge is required.
The objection relies on the idea that truth from a reliable source produces certainty for everyone.
2
Follow Baqillani's counter
Baqillani points out that accepting the objection means we must allow that today's Muslims could be lying about their chain. Consider the implications of allowing such a possibility.
3
Trace the absurd conclusion
If Muslims can be lying, then Jews, Christians, and all historical transmitters can also be lying. This leads to the nullification of all reports and all knowledge from them.
Baqillani calls this 'something from which there is no escape' – an unavoidable absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : لو كان ذلك كما يدعون لعلمنا صدقهم فيما نقلوه ضرورة قيل لهم : أوّل ما في هذا تجويزكم الكذب على عدد المسلمين اليوم في قولهم إنما أخذوا ذلك عن حجة وإن جاز الكذب عليهم في هذه الدعوى جاز عليهم في جميع ما يدعونه وينقلونه وجاز أيضاً على أمثالهم من اليهود والنصارى والمجوس ونقلة البلدان وفي ذلك التعطيل للأخبار والعلم بشيء من جهتها أصلاً وتجويز أن تكون اليهود اليوم كاذبة في قولها إن النقل أخذته عن حجة كَهِي وذلك ما لا خلاص منه .
If they say: If it were as they claim, we would have known their truthfulness necessarily in what they transmitted. Say to them: The first thing in this is your allowing lying against the multitude of Muslims today in their statement that they took that from an authoritative group; and if lying is allowed against them in this claim, it is allowed against them in all that they claim and transmit, and it is also allowed against their likes among Jews, Christians, Magians, and transmitters of cities, and in that is the nullification of reports and knowledge of anything from them at all, and allowing that the Jews today are lying in their statement that the transmission was taken from an authoritative group like them. And that is something from which there is no escape.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage contains a second objection and its refutation. The opponent argues that if the Muslim report of Muhammad's miracles were true, then the knowledge of its truthfulness would be necessary (darura)—that is, everyone would be compelled to accept it. Since not everyone accepts it, the report must be false. Baqillani responds by pointing out the consequences of this argument: the opponent's premise implies that it is permissible to accuse the entire multitude of Muslims today of lying when they claim to have received the report from an authoritative source. But if such a massive lie is possible, then lie is also possible against any similar group, including Jews, Christians, Magians, and all historical transmitters. This would nullify all historical reports (akhbar) and all knowledge derived from them. Furthermore, it would allow the possibility that the Jews today are also lying about their chain of transmission for the signs of Moses. Therefore, the objection leads to an absurd conclusion (تعطيل الأخبار) from which there is no escape, so the objection itself is invalid.

Key Terms
ضرورة (necessity)
تعطيل (nullification)
أخبار (reports)
حجة (authoritative group)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that for a report to be reliable, it must produce necessary knowledge that everyone accepts.
Correction: Baqillani shows that requiring necessary knowledge would make all reports unreliable, because if necessary knowledge were required, we could question any transmission, leading to the nullification of all historical knowledge.

Checkpoint
What consequence does Baqillani draw from the objector's demand that knowledge of transmission be necessary?
A) It proves Muslim transmission is false.
B) It would allow lying against all groups and nullify all reports.
C) It only applies to Jewish transmission.
D) It shows that Muslims are more trustworthy.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the objection by showing that requiring necessary knowledge from transmission would lead to the absurd nullification of all reports.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever require absolute certainty before accepting a truth about your faith? Has that demand ever prevented you from acting on reliable knowledge, even when it came from the Prophet (peace be upon him)?
?
Baqillani shows that doubting the Muslim transmission chain leads to doubting all history. In your own life, do you selectively doubt certain religious reports while easily accepting secular ones? What does that reveal about your heart's posture toward revelation?
?
If you were to apply the opponent's standard of necessary knowledge to every report you hear today, how would your daily life change? Would you still trust your parents, teachers, or even the news? What does that tell you about the necessity of trusting transmission?

The Self-Defeating Objection: Necessary Knowledge and Denial
Baqillani responds to the objection that opponents know the truth necessarily but deny it obstinately.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani turns the objection of necessary knowledge back against the objectors, demonstrating its self-defeating nature.

Core Idea
Claims of necessary knowledge can be reciprocated; denial does not disprove truth.

In this passage, Baqillani addresses a subtle objection: perhaps the opponents know the truth necessarily but are simply denying it out of obstinacy. This objection could weaken the reliability of their rejection. Baqillani's response is a masterful turn of the argument, showing that the same reasoning applies to the objectors themselves and leads to an infinite regress of subjective claims. You will see how parity logic dismantles the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Source Passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the flow of objection and response.
The passage is a dialogue; track each speaker.
2
Identify the Argument Structure
Break the passage into objection, counter, further objection, and final rebuttal. Notice how Baqillani uses parity with other religious groups.
The structure is: claim → counter → objection → parity refutation.
3
Reflect on the Logic
Why is the objection self-defeating? Consider how subjective denial does not refute a true report, and how the same logic can be applied to any group.
This shows the importance of objective criteria for knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : هم يعلمون ذلك ضرورةً ولكنهم يجحدون ما يعلمون قيل لهم : وكذلك أنتم وسائر النصارى عالمون بصحة نقل المسلمين لأعلام نبيهم غير أنكم تجحدون ذلك على علم منكم لصحته فإن قالوا: نجد أنفسنا بخلاف ما تدعون قيل لهم : وكذلك تزعم البراهمة والمجوس والفلاسفة وأهل الإلحاد أنهم يجدون أنفسهم غير عالمين بصحة نقلكم؛ فلم يجب تصديقكم؛ ولا جواب لهم ذلك .
If they say: They know that necessarily, but they deny what they know. Say to them: Likewise you and all the Christians know the correctness of the Muslims' transmission of the signs of their prophet, but you deny that knowingly of its correctness. If they say: We find ourselves contrary to what you claim. Say to them: Likewise the Brahmins, Magians, philosophers, and atheists claim that they find themselves not knowing the correctness of your transmission; therefore your truthfulness is not obliged, and there is no answer for them.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage addresses a further objection and its rebuttal. The opponents argue that they do know the truth of the Muslims' transmission necessarily, but they deliberately deny it out of obstinacy. Baqillani turns this against them: if that is their claim, then the same can be said of the Muslims and Christians regarding the Jewish transmission—they also know it necessarily but deny it. When the opponents object that they do not find themselves knowing it necessarily (they deny having such knowledge), Baqillani answers that the Brahmins, Magians, etc. also claim they do not find themselves knowing the Muslims' transmission necessarily. So, by the opponent's own standard, the Muslims' transmission is not proven false, and the opponents have no valid response. The argument shows that claims of necessary knowledge can be reciprocated, and subjective lack of awareness does not disprove the truth of a report.

Key Terms
ضرورة (necessity)
جحد (denial)
علم (knowledge)
وجدان (finding oneself)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That subjective denial or claim of ignorance disproves a true report.
Correction: Baqillani shows that if subjective denial invalidated a claim, then every group's denial would be equally valid, making proof impossible. Only objective criteria can refute a report.

Checkpoint
How does Baqillani respond when the opponents say they do not find themselves knowing the Muslim transmission necessarily?
A) He says they are lying.
B) He says that other groups also claim not to know the objectors' transmission necessarily.
C) He concedes the point.
D) He says necessary knowledge is not required.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani shows that claims of necessary knowledge can be reciprocated, and subjective lack of awareness does not invalidate a true report.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hesitate to accept a clear truth from revelation, do you examine whether that hesitation is rooted in evidence or in your own desire to avoid submission? How can Baqillani's argument help you distinguish between the two?
?
Have you ever dismissed a believer's testimony simply because you did not 'feel' it was true? What would it take for you to accept a truth even when your feelings resist?
?
If you find yourself denying a truth that you are fully aware of, what is the cost of that denial? Will you continue to let obstinacy block your path, or will you humble yourself before the truth?

The Dilemma of Jewish Transmission: From You or from Prophets?
Baqillani presents a dilemma to Jews regarding the source of their transmission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani presents a logical dilemma to Jewish opponents regarding the source of their religious transmission.

Core Idea
Jewish transmission forces an admission of Jesus and Muhammad's prophethood.

This lesson examines a powerful argument from Imam Baqillani. He presents a dilemma to Jewish interlocutors: either their religious transmission comes from their own people alone, which does not constitute a valid proof, or it comes from Jesus and Muhammad, which would mean acknowledging their prophethood. Read the passage and see how this forces a logical contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation. Notice the two possibilities presented.
The opening word 'السلام' may be a continuation from the previous page.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Find the two options: transmission from your own group, or transmission from Jesus and Muhammad. Notice how each horn leads to a problem.
3
Understand the logical force
Baqillani uses the Jews' own criteria about proof from a single group to argue against them. Consider how this dilemma is a powerful refutation.
This is not a threat but a logical argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السلام من أن يكون مأخوذاً في الأصل عنكم أو عن عيسى ومحمد اللذين لم يأخذا عنكم وإنما أخذا عن الله تعالى؟ فإن كانوا إنما أخذوا ذلك عنكم وأنتم في الأصل طبقة واحدة والحجة بقول الطبقة غير ثابتة وإن كانوا أخذوا ذلك عن عيسى ومحمد اللذين لم يأخذا عنكم فقد أخذا عن الله سبحانه وهذا إقرار منكم بنبوتهما
Peace. [Or: The question is:] Whether it is taken originally from you or from Jesus and Muhammad, who did not take from you but rather took from God Almighty? So if they took that from you while you are originally one group and the proof by the statement of the one group is not established, and if they took that from Jesus and Muhammad, who did not take from you, then they took from God Almighty, and this is an admission from you of their prophethood.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a dilemma posed by Imam Baqillani to Jewish opponents. He questions the origin of their religious transmission. He presents two possibilities: either the Jews received their religion from earlier Jewish generations, making them a single group whose unanimous statement is not a valid proof according to their own criteria, or they received it from the prophets Jesus and Muhammad (peace be upon them), who themselves received revelation from God. If the latter, then the Jews are admitting the prophethood of Jesus and Muhammad. This argument forces the Jews into a contradiction: they cannot claim the validity of their transmission without either undermining its authority or acknowledging other prophets.

Key Terms
نقل (transmission)
طبقة (generation/group)
حجة (proof)
إقرار (admission)
نبوة (prophethood)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is a simple threat or emotional appeal.
Correction: It is a logical dilemma based on the Jews' own premises about proof from a single generation.

Checkpoint
What are the two possibilities Baqillani presents for the source of Jewish transmission?
A) From their own ancestors or from previous prophets
B) From Moses or from some other book
C) From the Torah or from the Gospel
D) From revelation or from reason

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's dilemma shows that Jewish tradition cannot claim exclusive authority without either undermining itself or acknowledging other prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you rely on the scholars you follow simply because they are one group handed down from one group, or do you verify the chain back to the Prophet? Have you ever reconsidered a belief just because it was 'always taught' to you?
?
If someone presented a dilemma that forced you to admit a truth you resisted, would you submit or would you twist reasoning to avoid the conclusion? Where in your life are you avoiding an inevitable truth because of pride?
?
Your deen came to you through a chain of transmission. Have you ever truly thanked Allah for that chain, or do you take it for granted? What would it mean for you today if you personally had to hear the Prophet's ﷺ words as if for the first time?

Refuting the Dar Dhilla Criterion
Baqillani refutes the Jewish claim that transmission from the humiliated is reliable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani rejects the Jewish criterion of 'abode of humiliation' for reliable transmission and how he uses a reductio argument to show its inconsistency.

Core Idea
If humiliation guarantees truth, then Christians beat Jews — a self-destructive criterion.

In this passage, Baqillani confronts a Jewish claim that their transmission is uniquely reliable because they are in a state of humiliation and pay jizya. He does not accept this premise but instead turns it against them, showing that it would apply equally to Christians and undermine Jewish traditions. This refutation uses internal consistency, a hallmark of kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection: the Jewish claim
Notice how the Jews argue that their transmission is valid because they are in dar dhilla and pay jizya, implying they are not liars.
2
Identify the first counter: secular histories
Baqillani points out that if humiliation is the criterion, then transmitters of cities and histories would not be reliable since they are not from such people.
This shows the criterion is too narrow.
3
Follow the second counter: Christian transmission
The same criterion would make Christian transmission of Jesus' signs even more reliable, since Christians also pay jizya.
This creates an inconsistency for the Jews.
4
Understand the third counter: temporal absurdity
Before the Jews entered dar dhilla, the obligation to believe in Moses would not be valid, undermining their own religion.
This forces a dilemma.
5
Recognize the final dilemma
The Jews must either abandon their pretext or invalidate their own faith. Thus the criterion is self-defeating.
This is the conclusion of the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : إنما وجب صحة نقل اليهود لأنهم في دار ذلة الجزية وليس كذلك المسلمون لأنهم ليسوا في دار ذلة ولا ممن يؤدي الجزية فيقال لهم : فلا يجب على قولكم إثبات نقلة البلدان والسير لأنه ليس بوارد عن أهل ذلة وممن جزية؛ ويجب لهذه العلة صحة نقل النصارى لأعلام عيسى عليه السلام لأنهم في دار ذلة وممن يؤدي الجزية فلا يجدون من ذلك بداً أو يتركوا اعتلالهم ويقال لهم : فيجب سقوط فرض اعتقاد نبوة موسى عليه السلام وصحة ما جاء به قبل أن يحصلوا في دار ذلة وتؤخذ منهم الجزية فإن مروا على ذلك تركوا دينهم وإن أبوه أبطلوا هذا الشرط في صحة الأخبار
For if they say: The validity of the transmission of the Jews is necessary only because they are in the abode of humiliation and jizya, and the Muslims are not like that because they are not in an abode of humiliation nor among those who pay jizya, then it is said to them: According to your statement, it is not necessary to establish the transmitters of cities and histories because they are not reported from people of humiliation and those who pay jizya; and for this reason, the transmission of the Christians of the signs of Jesus, peace be upon him, must be sound because they are in an abode of humiliation and among those who pay jizya. So they will find no alternative but either to abandon their pretext, and it is said to them: Then the obligation of believing in the prophethood of Moses, peace be upon him, and the soundness of what he brought must fall before they entered the abode of humiliation and jizya was taken from them. If they passed over that, they would abandon their religion, and if they refused, they would invalidate this condition for the validity of reports.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage records an objection from the Jews and Baqillani's response. The Jews claim that their transmission is reliable because they are in a state of humiliation and pay jizya, implying that they would not lie under such conditions, whereas Muslims are not in such a state. Baqillani refutes this by pointing out that if this criterion is applied consistently, then firstly, the transmission of secular histories (like events in distant lands) would not be reliable, and secondly, the Christian transmission of the signs of Jesus (peace be upon him) would be even more reliable because Christians also pay jizya and are in humiliation. This forces the Jews into an absurdity: either abandon their claim, or else they must admit that before they entered the abode of humiliation, the obligation to believe in Moses was not valid, which would undermine their own religion. Thus, the proposed criterion is both self-defeating and leads to contradictions.

Key Terms
دار ذلة (abode of humiliation)
جزية (jizya)
نقل (transmission)
اعتلال (pretext/objection)
فرض (obligation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Baqillani is rejecting the Jewish claim by asserting the opposite (that humiliation makes transmission unreliable).
Correction: Baqillani does not deny that humiliation could be a factor; he shows that the criterion leads to absurdities if applied consistently, revealing the Jews' inconsistency.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani say about the Jews' claim that being in a state of humiliation makes their transmission reliable?
A) He accepts it and applies it to Muslims
B) He rejects it by showing it leads to absurdities and also validates Christian transmission
C) He argues that Muslims are also in humiliation
D) He says the claim is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the Jewish criterion for transmission by demonstrating that it leads to contradictions and undermines their own religious obligations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever clung to a flawed argument to defend a belief simply because it was convenient, rather than seeking the truth? Will you today search for a consistent criterion for your beliefs?
?
This refutation shows that a criterion that works for you must work for others too. Is there any belief you hold that you resist applying consistently to others? What would change if you did?
?
Baqillani forces the Jews to see that their own religious obligation would be invalidated by their criterion. Are you willing to examine whether any of your own religious positions, when applied consistently, would undermine other parts of your faith? What would you do if you found such a contradiction?

Turning the Tables: The Jews' Own Criterion Used Against Them
Baqillani refutes a Jewish objection by turning their own criterion against them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the Jews' own standard of transmission to nullify their objection against the Muslim and Christian transmission of prophetic signs.

Core Idea
Baqillani turns the Jews' criterion against them: if one group's report is invalid, their own transmission of Moses is also invalid.

Baqillani continues his dialectical refutation by showing that the Jews' objection to Muslim transmission actually undermines their own tradition. He first argues that Christian and Muslim transmissions are valid by the Jews' own standard of consensus among humiliated groups. Then, when the Jews object that Muslims merely took transmission from them, Baqillani turns the argument back: the same logic would nullify the Jews' own transmission of Moses' signs, since it too originates from a single group. This passage is a masterclass in logical consistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Pay attention to the back-and-forth structure.
2
Identify the argument structure
Notice the three parts: Baqillani's initial claim, the Jewish objection, and Baqillani's rebuttal. Label each part.
3
Reflect on the dialectical strategy
Ask yourself: How does Baqillani use the Jews' own premises against them? What is the key move?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم : فيجب صحة أعلام المسيح لإطباقهم والعيسوية ونحن معهم على نقلها لأن الفريقين يؤدون جزية وهي في دار ذلة وكذلك يجب صحة نقل المسلمين لأعلام محمد عليه السلام لإطباق العيسوية على نقلها وهم أهل ذلة وممن يؤدي الجزية فإن قالوا عنكم أخذوا هذا النقل وأنتم في الأصل فرقة واحدة قيل لهم : وكذلك المسلمون والنصارى ومحمد وعيسى عليهما السلام إنما أخذوا النقل لأعلام موسى عنكم وأنتم في الأصل طبقة واحدة ونقل الفرقة عندكم لا تقوم به الحجة فبطل نقلكم
And it is said to them: Then the soundness of the signs of the Messiah must be established due to the consensus of them (the Christians) and we with them upon their transmission, because the two groups pay jizya and they are in an abode of humiliation. Similarly, the soundness of the transmission of the Muslims of the signs of Muhammad, peace be upon him, must be established due to the consensus of the Christians upon their transmission, and they are people of humiliation and among those who pay jizya. If they say: They (the Muslims) took this transmission from you, while you are originally one group, it is said to them: Similarly, the Muslims and the Christians and Muhammad and Jesus, peace be upon them, only took the transmission of the signs of Moses from you, while you are originally one group, and the transmission of the one group according to you does not constitute proof, so your transmission is nullified.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage continues Baqillani's refutation. He first argues that Christian transmission of Jesus' signs must be accepted by the Jews' own criterion, and similarly Muslim transmission of Muhammad's signs, since Christians (and Muslims indirectly) are in humiliation and pay jizya. The Jews then object that the Muslims merely took this transmission from the Jews, so it is just one group's report. Baqillani turns this back: the transmission of the signs of Moses (peace be upon him) by Muslims, Christians, and the prophets themselves is also taken from the Jews, so by the same logic, the Jews' own transmission of Moses' signs is nullified because it is the report of one group, which they themselves claim does not constitute proof. Thus, their criterion is used against them.

Key Terms
إطباق
نقل الفرقة
حجة
بطلان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Baqillani is making a historical claim about where Muslims got their transmission.
Correction: Baqillani is using a hypothetical dialectical argument to expose inconsistency in the Jewish position, not asserting historical facts.

Checkpoint
How does Baqillani respond to the Jewish objection that Muslims took the transmission from them?
A) He admits it and gives up
B) He argues that the Jews themselves took the transmission of Moses from the same source, so it's also invalid
C) He says Muslims have independent transmission
D) He ignores the objection

Key Takeaway
Baqillani nullifies the Jewish objection by showing that the same criterion would invalidate the Jews' own transmission of Moses' signs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you defend your own beliefs, do you ever use standards of evidence that you would not accept if they were used against you? Consider the Jews in this lesson: they demanded a certain criterion for others, but Baqillani showed it backfired. Is there a standard you apply to others but would resist if applied to you? Will you examine your own position with the same rigor you use on others?
?
Baqillani did not fight the Jews’ criterion; he accepted it and turned it. In your spiritual life, are you willing to accept the full implications of the principles you claim to hold, even if they make you uncomfortable? If a principle you hold could be used to undermine something you cherish, would you rather abandon the principle or find an escape? How does your answer reveal your sincerity?
?
The Jews thought their argument was strong, but Baqillani’s move was surgically precise. Could your own confidence in certain religious positions be based on criteria that, if consistently applied, would also invalidate what you take for granted? Are you ready to sit with that possibility, or will you push it away? What would it take for you to honestly ask: 'Do I hold that only because it suits me?'

Refuting Testimony upon Testimony in Prophetic Proofs
Baqillani refutes the Jews' use of Christian testimony to support their transmission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani rejects the Jewish argument that combined Muslim and Christian testimony to Moses' signs constitutes independent proof for their claims.

Core Idea
Testimony based on another's testimony is not independent proof.

In polemical exchanges about prophetic authenticity, each community appeals to witnesses. The Jews here claim that both Muslims and Christians affirm the signs of Moses, while no one outside Islam affirms Muhammad's signs. Baqillani unpacks the epistemological flaw in this argument: the testimonies are not independent but are rooted in the Jews' own transmission. Let's examine how he turns their logic against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the Jewish objection
Read the first part of the passage where the Jews present their argument: both Muslims and Christians testify to Moses' signs, while no one testifies to Muhammad's signs. Consider why they think this is a strong point.
The Jews are using the consensus of two communities to bolster their claim.
2
Analyze Baqillani's first response
Notice how Baqillani responds: the Muslim and Christian testimonies are actually testimony upon the Jews' own testimony, because they rely on Jewish sources. Since the Jews are one group, multiple testimonies from a single original do not count as separate proof.
3
Follow the counter-argument
Read the second part where Baqillani applies the same logic to Jesus: Muslims and Christians also testify to Jesus' signs, so the Jews should accept that. See how the Jews' own objection would apply to their rejection of Jesus.
This is a classic 'what is good for the goose is good for the gander' argument.
4
Reflect on the principle
Ask yourself: what is the underlying principle about testimony that Baqillani is using? How does this principle apply in legal and theological contexts?
The key is the distinction between direct testimony and testimony based on another's testimony.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : قد شهدتم وشهدت النصارى لنا بصحة أعلام موسى وذلك كالبينة على دعوانا ولم نشهد لكم بصحة أعلام نبيكم، قيل لهم : وشهاداتنا وشهادة النصارى هي شهادة على شهادتكم وأنتم في الأصل فرقة واحدة وكثرة الشهادات على شهادة واحدة من واحد أو فرقة واحدة ليست بحجة ولا بينة ثم يقال لهم : وكذلك قد شهدنا نحن والعيسوية بصحة أعلام عيسى عليه السلام فيجب إثباتها عندكم فإن قالوا : شهادتكم على ذلك شهادة على شهادتهم وهي شهادة واحدة في الأصل قيل لهم : مثل ذلك فيما تعلقوا به
For if they say: You (Muslims) and the Christians have testified for us to the soundness of the signs of Moses, and that is like a clear proof for our claim, and we have not testified for you to the soundness of the signs of your prophet, it is said to them: Our testimony and the testimony of the Christians is a testimony upon your testimony, while you are originally one group, and the multiplicity of testimonies upon a single testimony from one person or one group is not a proof nor a clear evidence. Then it is said to them: Similarly, we and the Christians have testified to the soundness of the signs of Jesus, peace be upon him, so its establishment with you is necessary. If they say: Your testimony upon that is a testimony upon their testimony, and it is one testimony in origin, it is said to them: The same applies to what they cling to.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage addresses a further Jewish objection. The Jews point out that both Muslims and Christians acknowledge the validity of Moses' signs, which serves as independent proof for their claim, while no one acknowledges Muhammad's signs from non-Muslims. Baqillani responds that the testimonies of Muslims and Christians are actually testimonies upon the Jews' own testimony, because they rely on Jewish sources. Since the Jews are one group, multiple testimonies based on a single original testimony do not constitute proof. He then turns the argument: similarly, Muslims and Christians also testify to the signs of Jesus, so the Jews should accept that. If the Jews object that this is testimony upon Christian testimony, Baqillani says it is the same structure as their own argument, so it is consistent.

Key Terms
شهادة
بينة
دعوى
شهادة على شهادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the Jews have a valid point because multiple groups agreeing on Moses' signs seems like strong evidence.
Correction: Baqillani shows that the agreement is not independent; it is rooted in the Jews' own tradition, so it does not constitute separate proof.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani consider the Jews' use of Christian testimony invalid?
A) Because Christians are not reliable
B) Because the testimony is based on the Jews' own testimony, so it is not independent
C) Because Muslims do not accept Christian testimony
D) Because there is only one witness

Key Takeaway
Testimony that derives from another's testimony does not add independent evidentiary weight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Think of a time you accepted a claim because many people repeated it, without checking its original source. How does Baqillani's refutation of the Jews challenge you to examine the foundations of what you believe and teach?
?
This passage shows that even well-intentioned testimony can be worthless if it originates from a single source. In your life, where might you be building your faith or arguments on 'testimony upon testimony' rather than direct evidence? What will you do to change that starting today?

Refuting Compulsion by the Sword
Baqillani denies that Muslim transmitters were compelled by the sword.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's refutation of the claim that Muslim transmitters were compelled by the sword and his demand for evidence.

Core Idea
Claims of compulsion without evidence are baseless.

In this passage, Imam al-Baqillani addresses a Jewish objection that the transmission of Prophet Muhammad's signs is unreliable because Muslims were compelled by the sword. He challenges this claim, asking for proof and asserting that no compulsion occurred. The text cuts off, but the argument is clear: the burden of proof lies on the claimant.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the Jewish claim that Muslim transmitters of Muhammad's signs were compelled by the sword, unlike transmitters of Moses' signs.
The objection implies a double standard in evaluating reliability.
2
Identify Baqillani's counter-questions
Notice how Baqillani responds by asking for evidence and denying the claim outright.
He uses rhetorical questions to highlight the lack of proof.
3
Recognize the argument's structure
The passage follows an objection-response pattern, then cuts off. Reflect on how this refutation fits into broader debates about prophetic signs.
The incomplete ending suggests a continuation of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : إنما وجب القول بثبوت أعلام موسى عليه السلام لأن الناقلة لها لم يحملوا على نقلهم بالسيف ونقلة أعلام محمد عليه السلام محمولون على النقل بالسيف، قيل لهم : ولم زعمتم أنا محمولون على النقل للأعلام بالسيف وما دليلكم على ذلك وما أنكرتم أن تكون هذه الدعوى كذبا لأننا لم نحمل أحداً أسلم وأقر بالشهادتين على نقل أعلام نبينا عليه السلام؟ ولو اعترض معترض جمهور الأمة لم نجد عندها
For if they say: The statement of the establishment of the signs of Moses, peace be upon him, is necessary only because their transmitters were not compelled to transmit by the sword, while the transmitters of the signs of Muhammad, peace be upon him, are compelled to transmit by the sword, it is said to them: Why do you claim that we are compelled to transmit the signs by the sword? And what is your evidence for that? And what prevents it from being a lie, because we did not compel anyone who accepted Islam and confessed the two shahadas to transmit the signs of our prophet, peace be upon him? And if an objector objected to the majority of the ummah, we would not find with it...
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage records a Jewish objection that the transmission of Moses' signs is reliable because the transmitters were not compelled by the sword, whereas Muslim transmitters of Muhammad's signs were compelled. Baqillani rejects this as a baseless claim. He asks for evidence that Muslims were compelled, and denies that any such compulsion occurred. He states that Muslims did not force anyone who embraced Islam and recited the shahada to transmit the signs of the Prophet. The passage cuts off in mid-sentence, likely continuing to argue that if someone objects to the majority of the community, we do not find evidence for compulsion. Thus, the Jewish claim is dismissed as unfounded.

Key Terms

Correction: Baqillani clarifies that transmission of prophetic signs was voluntary; conversion and transmission are separate acts, and the claim of compulsion lacks evidence.

The Jewish Condition for Tawatur: Uncountable and Unbounded
Reports a Jewish condition for tawatur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the condition that some Jews placed on mass-transmitted reports (tawatur) and why it was considered an excessive standard by Muslim theologians.

Core Idea
Some Jews claimed only uncountable, unbounded reports yield certainty.

In discussing the conditions for knowledge-producing reports, Imam al-Baqillani reports a claim made by many Jews. They insisted that for a report to be tawatur (mass-transmitted) and yield certain knowledge, the transmitters must be beyond counting and not confined to any single country. This lesson examines that condition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that this is a reported claim, not the author's own view.
The text cuts off, so we only have the beginning of the claim.
2
Identify the condition asserted
Find the two requirements: (1) transmitters cannot be counted, (2) they cannot be limited to one country. Why would this be considered an extreme condition?
3
Understand the theological context
Reflect on why such a condition would make it impossible to accept the transmission of miracles (like those of Prophet Muhammad).
The author likely refutes this, but the text is incomplete.
4
Recognize the distinction between reporting and endorsing
Notice that al-Baqillani is simply reporting this view; it is not his own position. This is important to avoid misunderstanding.
The common misunderstanding is to attribute this claim to the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد زعم كثير من اليهود أن من شرط الخبر الموجب للعلم القاطع للعذر أن تكون الناقلة لا يحصرهم عدد ولا يحيوهم بلد ولا يجوز
And many of the Jews have claimed that among the conditions of the report that necessitates certain knowledge and cuts off excuse is that the transmitters are not limited by number, nor encompassed by a country, nor permissible...

The text breaks off, but the implication is that this is an excessive condition designed to reject the transmission of Muhammad's miracles. The author likely goes on to refute this condition, but the page ends.

Refutation of the Claim of Necessity in Transmitted Reports
Bāqillānī refutes the claim that knowledge of transmitted statements is necessary by a conditional reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Bāqillānī's logical reductio argument that a claim of universal necessity in transmitted reports collapses under its own implications, as it would force the Muslim community to be liars and thus destroy the entire system of tawātur.

Core Idea
A claim of universal necessity that contradicts universal denial undermines all transmitted knowledge.

This passage is a masterclass in logical refutation. Bāqillānī takes the Jewish claim that knowledge of certain transmitted statements is necessary for all who hear them and shows that it leads to an absurd consequence. If the claim were true, then the entire Muslim community—having heard the same transmission—would be compelled to know its truth. But Muslims collectively deny being compelled. This creates a devastating dilemma: either the Muslims are lying about their denial, or the claim of necessity is false. Bāqillānī then demonstrates that if the Muslims can be liars in this instance, then their entire transmission could be unreliable, which invalidates the principle of tawātur that all religious communities depend on.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure starting with 'If this necessity were correct...'.
The passage is a single continuous argument, so pay attention to the logical flow.
2
Identify the conditional premise
Find the first conditional: 'If the claimed necessity were true, then Muslims would be compelled to know.' Understand that this sets up the argument.
This is the hypothetical starting point.
3
Recognize the observed contradiction
Notice that Muslims deny being compelled. This contradicts what would follow if the claim were true. Recognize that Bāqillānī uses this to create a dilemma.
The denial is a fact, not an assumption.
4
Trace the consequences
Follow the reasoning: if Muslims are liars for denying compulsion, then they could lie about all transmissions, which invalidates tawātur. See how the argument extends to other nations and transmitters.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
5
Conclude the refutation
The final sentence shows the forced choice: either persist and abandon religion, or reject the claim. Understand that Bāqillānī has shown the claim cannot stand.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقال لهم : ولو كانت هذه الضرورة التي تدَّعونها صحيحة ثابتة وقد سمع المسلمون بنقلها كما سمعتم لوجب أن يكونوا مضطرين إلى العلم بصحتها وأن تكون حالهم في العلم بذلك كحالهم ولو كان ذلك كذلك لوجب أن يكون المسلمون مع كثرة عددهم وامتناع التراسل والتَّشَاعُرِ عليهم قد كَذَّبوا في قولهم : إنا غير عالمين بذلك ولا مضطرين إليه لأنهم عندكم مضطرون إليه ولو جاز عليهم الكذب على أنفسهم في جحد ما هم إلى العلم به مضطرون لجاز عليهم الكذب على غيرهم ولجاز أن يكونوا كَذَبَةً في سائر ما نقلوه ولجاز مِثْلُ الجائز عليهم على سائر الأمم من أهل الملل ونَقَلَةِ البُلْدَانِ وهذا يُبْطِلُ التواتُرَ جملةً فإن مضوا على ذلك تركوا دينهم وإن أبوا أبطلوا دعواهم.
It is said to them: If this necessity which you claim were correct and established, and the Muslims had heard its transmission as you heard, then they would have to be compelled to knowledge of its truth, and their state in knowledge of that would be like your state. And if that were the case, then it would be necessary that the Muslims, despite their large number and the impossibility of correspondence and mutual awareness [?] among them, would have lied in their saying: 'We are not knowledgeable of that nor compelled to it,' because according to you they are compelled to it. And if lying about themselves in denying what they are compelled to knowledge of is possible for them, then lying about others would be possible for them, and it would be possible that they are liars in all that they transmitted, and the same would be possible for the other nations of people of religions and transmitters of lands. And this invalidates tawātur (recurrent transmission) entirely. For if they persist in that, they abandon their religion, and if they refuse, they invalidate their claim.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (radd) of the Jewish claim that knowledge of certain transmitted statements is necessary (ḍarūrī). The author employs a conditional argument: if the claimed necessity were true, then Muslims, having heard the same transmission, would also be compelled to know its truth. But the Muslim community collectively denies being compelled. This creates a dilemma: either the Muslims are lying about their denial, or the claim of necessity is false. If the Muslims are liars in this, then they could be liars in all their transmitted reports, which would undermine the entire system of tawātur (recurrent transmission) that all religious communities rely on. The author concludes that the Jews must either persist in their claim (thereby undermining their own religion, which depends on tawātur) or abandon it (thereby invalidating their argument).

Key Terms
ضرورة (ḍarūra)
تواتر (tawātur)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge of certain transmitted statements is necessary (ḍarūrī) for all who hear them, regardless of their response.
Correction: Bāqillānī shows that if it were necessary, all hearers would be compelled to know it, but the Muslim community's denial of compulsion proves that either the claim is false or the entire system of transmission is unreliable, which is absurd.

Checkpoint
According to Bāqillānī, what would follow if the Muslim community were compelled to know the truth of the Jewish claim about Moses' statement?
A) Muslims would be wrong.
B) Muslims would be liars for denying it.
C) The Jewish claim would be proven true.
D) The Muslims would not need to transmit it.

Key Takeaway
Bāqillānī's reductio shows that a claim of universal necessity in transmission is self-defeating because it forces the entire community of transmitters to be liars, which destroys the very basis of reliable transmission (tawātur).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a claim about Islam that you haven't verified, do you feel compelled to accept it? This passage shows that false claims of necessity can undermine all trust in transmission. Have you ever accepted something as 'necessarily true' without evidence, and how did that affect your faith? What will you do the next time you encounter such a claim?
?
The argument reveals that if we allow even one false claim of necessity, it can unravel the entire basis of reliable knowledge. Are there areas in your life where you've accepted 'common knowledge' without scrutiny, and what might be at stake if that knowledge were false? How can you protect yourself from such uncritical acceptance?
?
Bāqillānī forces us to choose: either abandon religion or reject the false claim. What false certainties might you be holding onto that, if left unchallenged, could lead you away from the truth? Are you willing to examine them honestly and let go of what does not stand up to scrutiny?

The Rational Objection to Abrogation
Baqillani presents the deniers' rational argument against abrogation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument used by abrogation deniers to claim that abrogation implies foolishness in God.

Core Idea
Abrogation deniers argue that God commanding then prohibiting the same thing entails foolishness.

Some denied the possibility of abrogation based on a rational argument. They claimed that God's command must be for benefit and His prohibition for harm, so if He later prohibits what He once commanded, He must be acting foolishly. This lesson presents their reasoning carefully, so we can understand what exactly they claimed before seeing how it is refuted.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage from Baqillani that presents the deniers' argument. Notice how it begins with a question.
2
Identify the premises
The argument has two premises: (1) command implies benefit, prohibition implies harm; (2) abrogation involves prohibition of a previously commanded act. Work through the logic.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that if God abrogates, He must be foolish, which is impossible, so abrogation is impossible. Grasp why they think this follows.
This is the core of their objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام على محيل النسخ منهم من جهة العقل يقال لمن قال ذلك منهم لم قلتم هذا وما دليلكم عليه فإن قالوا لأن أمره بالشيء يقتضي كونه مصلحة والنهي عنه يقتضي كونه مفسدة فإذا نهانا عما أمرنا به وجب أن يكون سفيهاً إما في أمره بالفساد أو في نهيه عن الصلاح لأن ما نهي عنه بعد أمره به لا بد أن يكون صلاحاً أو فساداً فلما لم يكن أن يكون الباري سبحانه سفيهاً غير حكيم لم يجز نهيه عما كان أمر به يقال لهم ما أنكرتم إن كان ما قلتموه من ذلك صحيحاً على تسليم ما بنيتموه وإن كنا لا نقول به أن يكون ذلك إنما يقتضي أن يكون النهي عن نفس المأمور به قبل امتثاله على وجه أمر به يوجب ما قلتم وأن يكون ناهياً عن فعل المصلحة وألا يوجب إحالة نهيه عن نفس ما أمر به إحالة نهيه عن مثله بعد فعله لأن مثله الذي من سبيله أن يقع بعده هو غيره والنهي عن غير الشيء في غير وقته لا يكون نهيا عنه في وقته ولا جواب لهم عن ذلك
Chapter on discourse regarding those among them who deny abrogation from the rational perspective. It is said to one who says that from among them: Why did you say this, and what is your proof for it? For if they say: Because His command regarding a thing necessitates it being a benefit, and His prohibition of it necessitates it being a corruption. So if He prohibits us from what He commanded us, He must be foolish, either in His command of corruption or in His prohibition of benefit, because what is prohibited after being commanded must be either a benefit or a corruption. So since it is not possible that the Creator, glorified, be foolish and not wise, it is not permissible for Him to prohibit what He had commanded. It is said to them: What do you deny – if what you said of that is correct, accepting your assumption, although we do not hold it – that that only necessitates that the prohibition of the very thing commanded before its compliance in the manner commanded entails what you said, and that He is prohibiting the performance of benefit, but it does not entail the impossibility of His prohibition of the very thing He commanded being the same as His prohibition of its like after its performance, because its like which is of the way to occur after it is other than it, and the prohibition of other than a thing at a time other than its time is not a prohibition of it at its time. And they have no answer to that.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a rational argument and refutation regarding the possibility of abrogation (naskh). The author first presents the objection of those who deny abrogation: they argue that God's command implies benefit and His prohibition implies corruption. If God prohibits what He previously commanded, then He must be foolish (safih) because either His original command was for corruption or His later prohibition is against benefit. Since God is wise, abrogation is impossible. Baqillani then gives the answer: even if we accept their premise (for the sake of argument), the contradiction only applies if the prohibition is on the very same act before it is performed. However, abrogation prohibits the act after its time, and what is later prohibited is not the identical act but its like (mithl). The prohibition of something else at a different time is not a prohibition of the original act at its own time. Therefore, no contradiction or foolishness arises. Baqillani concludes that the deniers have no response to this.

Key Terms
نسخ
مصلحة
مفسدة
سفه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abrogation implies a change of mind or contradiction in God.
Correction: The deniers argue that abrogation necessarily implies foolishness, but this is based on a misunderstanding of the object of prohibition.

Checkpoint
According to the deniers' argument in this passage, what does God's command of a thing necessitate?
A) That it is a corruption
B) That it is a benefit
C) That it is foolish
D) That it is abrogated

Key Takeaway
The deniers' argument against abrogation is based on the assumption that command=benefit and prohibition=harm, leading to the charge of foolishness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt troubled by the idea that God's commands might change through abrogation? Does it shake your trust in His perfect wisdom? Will you commit today to study the wisdom behind naskh and deepen your reliance on His unchanging knowledge?
?
The deniers assumed that what is beneficial now must always be beneficial. Is there a command of God that you have struggled to follow because you assumed it no longer applies or seems outdated? Will you ask Allah to grant you the humility to submit even when you don't see the full picture?
?
When you encounter a ruling in the Qur’an that seems to contradict another, do you immediately doubt its consistency, or do you pause and trust that there is wisdom beyond your understanding? What step will you take this week to strengthen your conviction in the perfection of Allah's revelation?
