Baqillani's Refutation: The Distinction of Time and Instance
Baqillani refutes the deniers by distinguishing between the same act and its like.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's key response to the abrogation deniers: that abrogation prohibits a different instance of the act (its 'like') at a later time, not the original act at its time.

Core Idea
Abrogation prohibits the like of the act afterwards, not the identical act at its time.

After presenting the deniers' argument, Baqillani offers a precise refutation. He accepts their premise for argument's sake but shows that the supposed contradiction only arises if God prohibits the very same act before it is performed. But abrogation prohibits a similar act that occurs later, not the original commanded act at its own time. This distinction dissolves the charge of foolishness.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read Baqillani's answer from 'يقال لهم' to the end. Notice how he accepts their premise ('على تسليم ما بنيتموه').
2
Identify the key distinction
Baqillani distinguishes between prohibiting the very same act before compliance, and prohibiting its like (مثله) after it has been performed. Find this distinction in the text.
The word 'مثله' is crucial.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since the prohibition is of something else (غير) at a different time, it does not contradict the original command. The deniers have no answer.
Baqillani ends with 'ولا جواب لهم عن ذلك'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام على محيل النسخ منهم من جهة العقل يقال لمن قال ذلك منهم لم قلتم هذا وما دليلكم عليه فإن قالوا لأن أمره بالشيء يقتضي كونه مصلحة والنهي عنه يقتضي كونه مفسدة فإذا نهانا عما أمرنا به وجب أن يكون سفيهاً إما في أمره بالفساد أو في نهيه عن الصلاح لأن ما نهي عنه بعد أمره به لا بد أن يكون صلاحاً أو فساداً فلما لم يكن أن يكون الباري سبحانه سفيهاً غير حكيم لم يجز نهيه عما كان أمر به يقال لهم ما أنكرتم إن كان ما قلتموه من ذلك صحيحاً على تسليم ما بنيتموه وإن كنا لا نقول به أن يكون ذلك إنما يقتضي أن يكون النهي عن نفس المأمور به قبل امتثاله على وجه أمر به يوجب ما قلتم وأن يكون ناهياً عن فعل المصلحة وألا يوجب إحالة نهيه عن نفس ما أمر به إحالة نهيه عن مثله بعد فعله لأن مثله الذي من سبيله أن يقع بعده هو غيره والنهي عن غير الشيء في غير وقته لا يكون نهيا عنه في وقته ولا جواب لهم عن ذلك

For if they say: Because His command regarding a thing necessitates it being a benefit, and His prohibition of it necessitates it being a corruption. So if He prohibits us from what He commanded us, He must be foolish, either in His command of corruption or in His prohibition of benefit, because what is prohibited after being commanded must be either a benefit or a corruption. So since it is not possible that the Creator, glorified, be foolish and not wise, it is not permissible for Him to prohibit what He had commanded. It is said to them: What do you deny – if what you said of that is correct, accepting your assumption, although we do not hold it – that that only necessitates that the prohibition of the very thing commanded before its compliance in the manner commanded entails what you said, and that He is prohibiting the performance of benefit, but it does not entail the impossibility of His prohibition of the very thing He commanded being the same as His prohibition of its like after its performance, because its like which is of the way to occur after it is other than it, and the prohibition of other than a thing at a time other than its time is not a prohibition of it at its time. And they have no answer to that.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a rational argument and refutation regarding the possibility of abrogation (naskh). The author first presents the objection of those who deny abrogation: they argue that God's command implies benefit and His prohibition implies corruption. If God prohibits what He previously commanded, then He must be foolish (safih) because either His original command was for corruption or His later prohibition is against benefit. Since God is wise, abrogation is impossible. Baqillani then gives the answer: even if we accept their premise (for the sake of argument), the contradiction only applies if the prohibition is on the very same act before it is performed. However, abrogation prohibits the act after its time, and what is later prohibited is not the identical act but its like (mithl). The prohibition of something else at a different time is not a prohibition of the original act at its own time. Therefore, no contradiction or foolishness arises. Baqillani concludes that the deniers have no response to this.

Key Terms
مثله
مصلحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abrogation prohibits the identical act that was commanded at its original time.
Correction: Baqillani explains that abrogation prohibits a like act (مثله) occurring later, not the original act at its time.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani's argument, why does abrogation not imply foolishness?
A) Because God never makes mistakes
B) Because the prohibition is of a like act (مثله) at a different time
C) Because the deniers' premises are false
D) Because abrogation is not really a prohibition

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the deniers by showing that abrogation prohibits a different act (the like of the commanded act) at a later time, not the original commanded act at its time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you correct a mistake in your own judgment, do you ever feel you are being inconsistent? How can Baqillani's precision—distinguishing between the same act and its like—help you extend grace to yourself and others when you change your mind about a command or rule? What would it look like for you to apply this distinction before accusing yourself of hypocrisy?
?
Consider a time you held someone accountable for an action that was 'like' a previous one but not identical. Did you treat it as a fresh offense or as a repeat of the same? How does Baqillani's argument challenge the way you judge others? Will you commit to examining whether an action is truly the same or merely similar before passing judgment?
?
Baqillani shows that God's prohibition of a later, similar act does not contradict His earlier command. Is there an area in your life where you fear God's commands are contradictory—perhaps regarding a past sin and a present struggle? How does this lesson reassure you that God's prohibitions are always for new instances, not a condemnation of your past? What step can you take today to trust His consistency?

Second Rational Argument Refuted: Good Becomes Bad
Baqillani refutes the argument that abrogation makes good become bad, using the analogy of eating and drinking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the claim that abrogation turns good into bad by distinguishing between the act itself and its like, using everyday analogies.

Core Idea
The same type of act can be good or bad depending on time and circumstance.

In this passage, Baqillani continues his refutation of the deniers' rational objections to abrogation. They argue that if a command is later prohibited, the previous good becomes bad, which is impossible. Baqillani replies with a simple but powerful distinction: the prohibition applies to a like instance, not the identical act, and he supports this with analogies from daily life like eating and drinking.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage carefully to follow the flow of argument.
Notice the repetition of key terms like طاعة and معصية.
2
Identify the objection and Baqillani's answer
The deniers say abrogation makes obedience become disobedience. Baqillani says the prohibition is of the like (mithl), not the identical act.
3
Examine the analogy of eating and drinking
Baqillani gives concrete examples where the same action is good or bad depending on context. This illustrates that moral value is not fixed but contingent.
Think about how this applies to the acts of worship mentioned at the end.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الجواب هو جوابهم على اعتلالهم في هذا الباب أن الأمر بالشيء يقتضي كونه طاعة والنهي عنه يقتضي كونه إذا فعل معصية والطاعة لا يجوز أن تكون معصية لأن مثل الشيء إذا نهي بعد فعله فليس بنهي عنه فلم تصر الطاعة معصية وهو بعينه الجواب عن اعتلالهم بأن نسخ الشريعة بعد الأمر بها يوجب كون الحسن قبيحاً والحكمة سفها لأن الأمر بالشيء يقتضي كونه حكمة حسناً صواباً والنهي عنه يقتضي كونه قبيحاً عبثاً ولا يجوز أن يكون الحسن قبيحاً لأن النهي عن مثل الحسن المأمور به نهي عن غيره وليس يمتنع أن يكون مثل الصلاح في وقت فساداً في وقت آخر ومثل الطاعة في وقت معصية في وقت آخر ومثل الحسن في وقت قبيحاً في غيره ترى أن الأكل والشرب والعلاج بالكي طاعة حسن صواب مصلحة عند العطش والجوع وحدوث الأمراض المقتضية للعلاج وفعل ذلك أجمع عند الشبع والري والصحة والغنى عن التداوي قبيح وسفه ومعصية لله عز وجل فليس عند جميع العقلاء يمتنع أن تكون هذه العبادات السمعية نحو الصوم والصلاة والتوجه إلى بيت المقدس وترك العمل في السبت
And this answer is their answer to their argument in this chapter that the command of a thing necessitates it being obedience, and the prohibition of it necessitates it being, when done, disobedience. And obedience cannot be disobedience because the like of a thing, if prohibited after its performance, is not a prohibition of it, so obedience does not become disobedience. And it is the very answer to their argument that abrogation of the law after its command necessitates the good becoming bad and wisdom becoming foolishness, because the command of a thing necessitates it being wisdom, good, right, and the prohibition of it necessitates it being bad, vain. And it is not permissible for the good to become bad because the prohibition of the like of the good that was commanded is a prohibition of other than it. And it is not impossible that the like of benefit at one time be corruption at another time, and the like of obedience at one time be disobedience at another time, and the like of good at one time be bad at another. Do you not see that eating, drinking, and treatment by cauterization are obedience, good, right, benefit at the time of thirst, hunger, and the occurrence of illnesses requiring treatment, while doing all of that at the time of satiety, drinking to satisfaction, health, and independence from treatment is bad, foolish, and disobedience to God, mighty and majestic. So in the view of all rational people, it is not impossible that these revealed acts of worship such as fasting, prayer, facing toward Jerusalem, and refraining from work on Saturday...
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage extends the previous refutation to another version of the deniers' argument. They claim that if abrogation occurs, then an obedience becomes a disobedience, and good becomes bad, which is impossible. Baqillani replies that the same answer applies: the prohibition in abrogation is of the like (mithl) of the act, not the identical act. Therefore, the original act of obedience remains obedience; what is later prohibited is a different instance. He gives a vivid analogy: eating and drinking are beneficial and obedient when one is hungry or thirsty, but they become harmful and disobedient when one is already full or healthy. Similarly, medical cauterization is good when needed but bad when unnecessary. Just as rational people accept that the same type of act can be good or bad depending on time and circumstance, so too can acts of worship like fasting, prayer, facing Jerusalem, or Saturday observance be commanded at one time and prohibited at another without implying contradiction or injustice.

Correction: Baqillani shows that goodness is context-dependent; the same act can be good at one time and bad at another, like eating when hungry vs. full.

Distinguishing the Act from Its Like: Refuting Objections to Abrogation
Baqillani refutes two objections by distinguishing the act from its like.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes objections to abrogation by distinguishing between an act and its like, showing that later prohibitions do not contradict earlier commands.

Core Idea
Abrogation does not change the nature of an act; it only affects its like at another time.

In this passage, Imam al-Baqillani confronts two key objections against the possibility of divine abrogation. Opponents argue that abrogation would turn obedience into disobedience and good into evil, which is impossible. Baqillani counters by drawing a crucial distinction: the later prohibition applies to the 'like' of the act, not the act itself. This distinction preserves the consistency of God's commands and shows that the same type of action can have different moral statuses depending on context.

Guided Path
1
Read and note the repeated phrase
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice how often the term 'mithl' (like) appears. Underline each occurrence to see how it structures Baqillani's argument.
2
Identify the two objections and the same answer
The passage addresses two objections: 1) abrogation would turn obedience into disobedience, 2) it would turn good into evil. Baqillani applies the same reasoning to both: the prohibition is on the like, not the original act.
Notice the phrase 'وهو بعينه الجواب' indicating the same answer works for both.
3
Reflect on the real-world examples
Baqillani gives examples of eating, drinking, and cauterization to show that context changes moral evaluation. Reflect on how these examples clarify his argument.
This will help you see that the same action can be good or bad depending on time and circumstance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إحالة نهيه عن نفس ما أمر به إحالة نهيه عن مثله بعد فعله لأن مثله الذي من سبيله أن يقع بعده هو غيره والنهي عن غير الشيء في غير وقته لا يكون نهيا عنه في وقته؛ ولا جواب لهم عن ذلك. وهذا الجواب هو جوابهم على اعتلالهم في هذا الباب أن الأمر بالشيء يقتضي كونه طاعة والنهي عنه يقتضي كونه إذا فعل معصية والطاعة لا يجوز أن تكون معصية لأن مثل الشيء إذا نهي بعد فعله فليس بنهي عنه فلم تصر الطاعة معصية وهو بعينه الجواب عن اعتلالهم بأن نسخ الشريعة بعد الأمر بها يوجب كون الحسن قبيحا والحكمة سفها لأن الأمر بالشيء يقتضي كونه حكمة حسنا صوابا والنهي عنه يقتضي كونه قبيحا عبثا ولا يجوز أن يكون الحسن قبيحا لأن النهي عن مثل الحسن المأمور به نهي عن غيره وليس يمتنع أن يكون مثل الصلاح في وقت فسادا في وقت آخر ومثل الطاعة في وقت معصية في وقت آخر ومثل الحسن في وقت قبيحا في غيره. ترى أن الأكل والشرب والعلاج بالكي طاعة حسن صواب مصلحة عند العطش والجوع وحدوث الأمراض المقتضية للعلاج وفعل ذلك أجمع الشبع والري والصحة والغنى عن التداوي قبيح وسفه ومعصية لله عز وجل فليس يمتنع عند جميع العقلاء أن تكون هذه العبادات السمعية نحو الصوم والصلاة والتوجه إلى بيت المقدس وترك العمل في السبت مصلحة في وقت مفسدة في وقت طاعة وصوابا في وقت ومثلها معصية وسفها في وقت آخر وإذا كان ذلك كذلك بطل جميع ما يتعلقون به من هذه الأمور.
The impossibility of His prohibition of the very thing He commanded is the impossibility of His prohibition of its like after its occurrence, because its like which by its nature occurs after it is other than it, and the prohibition of something other than a thing at a time other than its time is not a prohibition of it at its time; and they have no answer to that. And this answer is their answer to their objection in this chapter that the command of a thing necessitates that it is obedience, and the prohibition of it necessitates that if it is performed it is disobedience, and it is not permissible for obedience to become disobedience, because if the like of a thing is prohibited after its occurrence, it is not a prohibition of it, so obedience does not become disobedience. And it is the very same answer to their objection that abrogation of the law after commanding it necessitates that good becomes evil and wisdom becomes foolishness, because the command of a thing necessitates that it is wisdom, good, and correct, and the prohibition of it necessitates that it is evil and vain, and it is not permissible for good to become evil, because the prohibition of the like of the commanded good is a prohibition of something else. And it is not prevented that the like of righteousness at one time be corruption at another time, and the like of obedience at one time be disobedience at another time, and the like of good at one time be evil at another. Do you not see that eating, drinking, and treatment by cauterization are obedience, good, correct, and beneficial at the time of thirst, hunger, and the occurrence of diseases requiring treatment, but doing all that when satiated, quenched, healthy, and free from need of treatment is evil, foolish, and disobedience to God, Mighty and Majestic. So it is not prevented in the view of all rational people that these revealed acts of worship, such as fasting, prayer, facing Jerusalem, and abstaining from work on Saturday, are beneficial at one time, corrupt at another, obedience and correct at one time, and their like are disobedience and foolish at another time. And if that is the case, all that they cling to from these matters becomes invalid.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two related objections to divine abrogation (naskh). The author begins by stating that the impossibility of God prohibiting the very act He commanded is actually the impossibility of prohibiting its like (mithl) after it occurs. The key distinction: the like of a thing at a later time is a different act, so prohibiting it does not contradict the earlier command. Then he applies this answer to the first objection: that if an act is commanded it is obedience, but if later prohibited it would become disobedience, which is impossible. The author explains that the prohibition of the like after the fact is not a prohibition of the original act, so obedience does not become disobedience. He states that the same answer refutes the second objection: that abrogation would make good (hasan) become evil (qabih). The prohibition of the like of a good commanded act is a prohibition of something else, not the good itself. The author then gives a concrete example: eating, drinking, and medical cauterization are good and beneficial when hungry, thirsty, or ill, but become evil and disobedience when one is already full, hydrated, and healthy. Thus, the same type of action can be good in one context and evil in another. He concludes that rational people accept that revealed acts of worship (like fasting, prayer, facing Jerusalem, and Sabbath observance) could be beneficial at one time and corrupt at another. Therefore, the opponents' objections are invalid.

نسخ (naskh)
مثل (mithl)
طاعة (ta'a)
معصية (ma'siya)
حسن (hasan)
قبيح (qabih)
مصلحة (maslaha)
مفسدة (mafsada)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think abrogation implies that God changes His mind or that the same act can be both obedience and disobedience at the same time.
Correction: Baqillani clarifies that the later prohibition applies to a different act (the like), so the original act remains unchanged in its time; moral status is context-dependent.

Checkpoint

A) Because God's commands are eternal and cannot be abrogated.
B) Because the like of a thing is a different act, and its prohibition does not apply to the original commanded act.
C) Because obedience and disobedience are purely subjective.
D) Because the prohibition occurs at the same time as the command.

Key Takeaway
The key to understanding abrogation without contradiction is recognizing that later rulings apply to the 'like' of an act, not the act itself, so the original act remains unchanged in its time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refuting the Contradiction Objection to Abrogation
Baqillani refutes the objection that abrogation implies contradictory desire and aversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the objection that abrogation implies contradictory desire and aversion in God.

Core Idea
Different times allow different divine judgments without contradiction.

In previous lessons, Baqillani refuted objections based on divine wisdom and justice. Now he addresses a third: that abrogation would imply God both desires and dislikes the same thing. The refutation hinges on distinguishing the act itself from its temporal context. This passage presents the objection, the answer, and a clarifying analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Get a sense of the argument's flow.
2
Identify the objection
The opponents argue that command indicates desire, prohibition indicates aversion, and both cannot coexist. Locate this in the text.
3
Understand the refutation
Baqillani answers that the desired act at one time is not identical to the disliked act at another; they are only similar (mithl). This avoids contradiction.
The key concept is mithl (likeness) – not identity.
4
Analyze the analogy
Consider the analogy of eating when hungry vs. eating when full. Why does this illustrate the refutation?
The analogy shows that the same action can be desired in one state and disliked in another.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Baqillani ends by asserting there is no valid answer to this refutation. Consider why this strengthens the case for abrogation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن هم قالوا الدليل على منع النسخ من جهة العقل أن الأمر بالشيء يدل على أنه مراد للأمر والنهي عنه يدل على كراهته ومحال أن يكون المراد كونه الله سبحانه مكروها مع كونه له مرادا أجيبوا بمثل ما تقدم لأن المراد في وقت هو غير مثله الذي يكره في وقت آخر؛ كما أن المراد من الأكل مع لهب الجوع غير المكروه منه مع البطنة والامتلاء والشبع التام ولا جواب عن ذلك.
And if they say: The rational proof for preventing abrogation is that the command of a thing indicates that it is desired (muraad) by the commander, and the prohibition of it indicates His aversion (karaahiyya) to it, and it is impossible for something to be both desired by God, exalted is He, and also an object of aversion while being desired by Him. They are answered with something similar to what preceded: because that which is desired at one time is other than its like which is disliked at another time, just as the desired aspect of eating with the flame of hunger is other than the disliked aspect of it with gluttony, repletion, and complete satiety. And there is no answer to that.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes a third rational objection to abrogation. The opponents argue that if God commands something, it indicates that He desires (yurid) it, and if He later prohibits it, that indicates He dislikes it. It is impossible for one thing to be both desired and disliked by God simultaneously. The author answers using the same principle as before: the thing desired at one time is not identical to the thing disliked at a later time; it is only its like (mithl). He gives an analogy: eating when extremely hungry (flame of hunger) is desired, but eating when already full (gluttony, repletion, complete satiety) is disliked. These are two different states of the same action; the second is not the same as the first but its like. Therefore, there is no contradiction in God desiring one instance and disliking its like at another time. The author concludes that there is no valid answer to this refutation.

Key Terms
مراد (muraad)
كراهة (karaaha)
إرادة (iraada)
مثل (mithl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abrogation implies God desires and dislikes the same thing at the same time.
Correction: The desired act and the disliked act are not identical but only alike; they occur at different times, so no contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the author, the analogy of eating when hungry versus eating when full demonstrates that:
A) Eating is always permissible.
B) The same action can be both desired and disliked in the same context.
C) The desired action and the disliked action are like each other, not identical.
D) Abrogation is impossible because it creates contradiction.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the contradiction objection by showing that divine desire is temporally indexed, so there is no conflict between a command and subsequent prohibition.

The opponents thought abrogation implied a change in God's desire. Silent introspection: Do you ever unconsciously imagine God as changeable like humans? How does this passage reshape your understanding of God's eternal will and its application to different times?

Refuting the Objection of Badā' in Abrogation
Baqillani refutes the objection that abrogation implies badā'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the claim that abrogation implies a change in God's knowledge (badā').

Core Idea
Abrogation prohibits the like of a command at a different time, not the original act.

This passage addresses a key objection: that abrogation would mean God changes His mind. Baqillani shows that when God abrogates a previous law, He prohibits a similar action at a later time, not the original command itself. Thus, God's knowledge remains unchanged.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the objection is presented and then refuted.
The objection uses the concept of badā' (change of intention).
2
Identify the key distinction
Find the phrase 'the like of what He had commanded' (مثل ما كان أمر به). This is crucial: the prohibition applies to a similar act at a different time, not the original command.
3
Understand the defense of God’s unchanging knowledge
Reflect on why this distinction preserves God's eternal knowledge. Since the original command remains beneficial in its time, and only its like is harmful later, no change in God's intention occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قالوا: الدليل على إحالة النسخ من جهة العقل أنه يوجب البداء لأن الأمر بالشيء يقتضي كونه مصلحة واعتقاد الأمر به كونه كذلك والنهي عنه بعد الأمر به يدل على أنه قد بدا للآمر وانكشف له أن ما كان أمر به مفسدة ليس بمصلحة على ما توهمه وذلك منتفي عن الله جل ذكره كان الجواب عنه أيضا ما تقدم وذلك أن الله تعالى إنما نهى لما نسخ شريعة موسى عن مثل ما كان أمر به وأن يفعل ذلك في وقت غير وقت ذلك المفعول الأول والنهي عن مثل الشيء في وقته ليس بنهي عنه.
And if they say: The rational proof for the impossibility of abrogation is that it necessitates badā' (change of intention) because the command of a thing implies that it is beneficial (maslaha) and the belief of the commander that it is so, and the prohibition of it after commanding it indicates that it has become apparent (badā) to the commander and it has been revealed to him that what he had commanded was harmful not beneficial, according to what they imagine. And that is negated of God, exalted be His mention. The answer about it is also what preceded: and that is that God, exalted, only prohibited when He abrogated the law of Moses the like of what He had commanded, and that it be done at a time other than the time of that first action, and the prohibition of the like of a thing at its time is not a prohibition of it.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage refutes a fourth rational objection: that abrogation implies badā' (بداء), meaning a change of decision or knowledge in God. The opponents argue that if God commands something as beneficial and later prohibits it, that would indicate that He first thought it beneficial and then discovered it was harmful, which is impossible for God. The author responds with the same underlying principle: when God abrogated the law of Moses, He prohibited the like (mithl) of what He had commanded, not the identical commanded act. The prohibition applies to a different time and a different instance, not to the original act. Therefore, there is no change in God's knowledge or decision; the commanded act remains beneficially correct in its time, and its like is harmful in another time. The prohibition of the like of a thing in its (the like's) time is not a prohibition of the original thing.

Key Terms
بداء (badā')
مصلحة (maslaha)
مفسدة (mafsada)
مثل (mithl)
نسخ (naskh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think abrogation implies that God learns new information or changes His mind.
Correction: Baqillani clarifies that abrogation concerns different instances at different times, not the same act, so God's knowledge remains unchanged.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, why does the abrogation of Moses' law not imply badā' in God?
A) Because God's knowledge is eternal and unchanging.
B) Because God prohibited the like of the commanded act at a different time, not the original command.
C) Because the prohibition occurred before the command was implemented.
D) Because badā' is impossible for anyone.

Key Takeaway
Abrogation does not imply badā' because God prohibits a similar act at a different time, not the original command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that God prohibits the 'like' of a command at a different time, how does this reality confront any hidden doubt you have about His wisdom or consistency in your own life? Will you trust His timing even when you cannot see the full picture?
?
Have you ever felt that God's commands seem contradictory to your current situation, leading you to question His guidance? How does this lesson's distinction between the original command and its 'like' in a different time challenge you to re-evaluate your perspective?
?
Reflect on a time when you were tempted to accuse God of changing His mind because a command felt difficult. Knowing now that abrogation is about different times and not a change in God, will you commit to obeying each command with full confidence in His unchanging wisdom, even when it is hard?

Divine Names Are Based on Revelation
Baqillani states that divine names like 'light', 'deceiver', 'mocker' are from revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that certain divine names are accepted only because they appear in revelation, even if their literal meanings seem incompatible with God's majesty when understood rationally.

Core Idea
Divine names are determined by revelation, not by human reasoning.

Imam Baqillani addresses a key principle in Islamic theology: the source of divine names. While reason can affirm some attributes, other names like 'deceiver' or 'mocker' are only used because Allah has revealed them. This lesson unpacks why the Law (shar') takes precedence over rational objections in naming Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice how Baqillani mentions human faculties before turning to the main point about divine names.
The passage begins with a comparison that sets up the discussion.
2
Understand the principle: revelation over reason
Identify that Baqillani acknowledges reason may forbid the literal meanings of names like 'deceiver' and 'mocker', but revelation permits the names themselves. The criterion for naming Allah is what the Law conveys.
This is the core theological point: sam' (revelation) is the source.
3
Realize the conclusion: focus on meaning, not names
Baqillani concludes that arguing over names is futile because the real discussion is about the meanings. The names are accepted as revealed; the meaning is understood in a manner befitting Allah.
This prevents futile debates over terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

So that indicates that what is considered in naming Him is what is conveyed by the Law and permission, not other than it. In summary, the discussion is about meaning, not the name, so there is no benefit in quibbling and clinging to discussion about names.

Commentary
This passage is a statement on the principle of divine names. Baqillani argues that certain names like 'light' (nur), 'deceiver' (makir), 'mocker' (mustahzi'), and 'scoffer' (sakhir) are used for God only because they appear in revelation (sam'), even though rational consideration might prevent attributing the literal meanings of these terms to God. Reason would deny that God deceives or mocks in the human sense. Thus, the criterion for naming God is what the Law (shar') has brought, not independent reasoning. He concludes that the discussion is about meanings, not names, so arguing over names is futile.

The Meaning of 'Muhdath Dhikr' in Quran 21:2
Baqillani explains the meaning of 'muhdath dhikr' in Quran 21:2 and refutes the implication that all revelation is created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Bāqillānī refutes the claim that the Quranic phrase 'mā ya'tīhim min dhikrin min rabbihim muḥdath' implies that all divine revelation is created.

Core Idea
The description 'muhdath' actually proves that not all dhikr is created.

Al-Bāqillānī addresses a key objection from those who argue that the Quran is created based on the verse 'There comes not to them a reminder from their Lord but it is newly originated.' In this passage, he provides multiple interpretations and a powerful linguistic argument to show that the verse refers only to temporal prophetic reminders, not the eternal Quran.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that this is a question-and-answer style (mas'ala). The questioner implies the verse supports the createdness of the Quran.
The passage is incomplete at the end due to OCR; focus on the argument as presented.
2
Identify Baqillani's main interpretations
Baqillani gives three interpretations: (1) 'dhikr' means the Prophet's admonitions, (2) it could refer to successive prophets, (3) the verse describes Quraysh's reaction as playing, though they were actually confounded. Pinpoint each one.
He also cites Quranic evidence like 'fa-dhakkir' (88:21) and 'dhikran rasūlan' (65:10-11).
3
Understand the linguistic argument
Baqillani argues that if all dhikr were muhdath, the description 'muhdath' would be redundant, just as saying 'a male man' is pointless. Thus, the verse implies some dhikr is not muhdath.
This is a key logical point: specifying a subset indicates there is another subset.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal defense
This argument protects the doctrine that the Quran is uncreated. The verse is about particular prophetic reminders, not the eternal speech of Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة. فإن قالوا: فما معنى قوله: ما يأتيهم من ذكر من ربهم محدث؟ قيل لهم: معناه ما يأتيهم من وعظ من النبي، صلى الله عليه، ووعد وتخويف إلا استمعوه وهم يلعبون. لأن وعظ النبي، صلى الله عليه، ووعيده وتحذيره ذكر. قال الله تبارك وتعالى: فذكر إنما أنت مذكر. ويقال: فلان في مجلس الذكر. وهذا أولى، لأن قريشاً لم تضحك وتلعب بالقرآن، ولكن أفحمت عند سماعه وتشتت فيه أهواؤهم وآراؤهم. ويحتمل أن يكون أراد: ما يأتيهم من نبي بعد نبي إلا استمعوا قوله ولعبوا وأعرضوا عنه. وقد سمى الله تعالى الرسول ذكراً، فقال: ذكرا رسولا يتلو عليكم آيات الله. وأيضاً فإن الله تعالى لم يقل: «ما يأتيهم من ذكر من ربهم إلا كان محدثاً». وفي الآية دلالة على أن في الذكر ما ليس بمحدّث لأجل نعته للذكر بالحدوث. ولو كان لا ذكر إلا محدث، لم يكن لقوله: (مِنْ ذِكْرٍ مِنْ رَبِّهِمْ مُحْدَثٍ معنى . كما أنه لا معنى لقول القائل: «ما يأتيني من رجل ذكر إلا أكرمته، ولا هاشمي شريف إلا قدمته»، إذا كان الرجل لا يكون
A question. If they say: What is the meaning of His saying: 'There comes not to them from a reminder (dhikr) from their Lord but it is newly originated (muhdath)?' It is said to them: Its meaning is: There does not come to them an admonition from the Prophet, peace be upon him, and a promise and a threat except they listen to it while they are playing. Because the admonition of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and his threat and his warning are a reminder (dhikr). Allah, Blessed and Exalted, said: 'So remind, for you are only a reminder' (Fa-dhakkir innamā anta mudhakkir). And it is said: 'So-and-so is in a gathering of remembrance.' And this is more appropriate, because Quraysh did not laugh and play with the Quran, but they were confounded at its hearing and their desires and opinions became scattered in it. And it is possible that He meant: There does not come to them a prophet after a prophet except they listened to his words and played and turned away from him. And if there were no reminder except newly-originated, there would be no meaning to His saying: 'from a reminder from their Lord newly originated' just as there is no meaning to a speaker's saying: 'There comes not to me a male man except I honor him, nor a noble Hashimite except I advance him,' if a man is not
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a question-and-answer (mas'ala) in which al-Bāqillānī addresses an objection concerning the Quranic verse 'mā ya'tīhim min dhikrin min rabbihim muḥdath' (Q. 21:2). The questioner implies that the verse indicates that all forms of divine reminder (dhikr) are 'newly originated' (muḥdath), which could be used to argue that the Quran itself is created. The author provides multiple interpretations to neutralize this implication. First, he explains that 'dhikr' here refers to the Prophet's preaching, promises, and threats, which listeners mock despite their being a form of remembrance. He supports this by citing 'fa-dhakkir innamā anta mudhakkir' (Q. 88:21), where the Prophet is called a 'reminder'. Second, he suggests that Quraysh's reaction was not laughter but bewilderment, making the interpretation of 'playing' less likely. Third, he posits that the verse might refer to prophets being sent successively, and the people playing and turning away. He further notes that Allah calls the messenger a 'dhikr' in Q. 65:10-11. Finally, he makes a linguistic argument: the verse specifies 'min dhikrin... muḥdath' (a newly-originated reminder), which implies that there is also a reminder that is not newly-originated. Otherwise, the description would be redundant, like saying 'I don't honor any male man' when all men are male. The answer thus upholds that not all divine communication is created; some (like the Quran itself) are eternal, while the 'dhikr' in this verse refers to temporal prophetic reminders.

Key Terms
ذكر محدث
وعظ
نبي
آيات الله
ذكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'mā ya'tīhim min dhikrin min rabbihim muḥdath' proves that the Quran is created because it says 'muhdath' (newly originated).
Correction: Baqillani shows that 'muhdath' describes the prophetic reminders that come to the disbelievers, not the eternal Quran itself. The very act of specifying 'muhdath' implies there is a category of dhikr that is not muhdath.

Checkpoint
How does al-Bāqillānī argue that the word 'muhdath' in Q. 21:2 does not imply that the Quran itself is created?
A) He says that 'dhikr' refers only to the Prophet's sayings, not the Quran.
B) He says that if all dhikr were muhdath, the description would be redundant, and the verse implies some dhikr is not muhdath.
C) He says that the verse is abrogated.
D) He says that 'muhdath' means 'destroyed' not 'created'.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Bāqillānī defends the uncreatedness of the Quran by showing that the phrase 'muhdath dhikr' in Q. 21:2 refers only to temporal prophetic reminders, and its very specificity proves that not all divine reminders are created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you truly approach it as the eternal, uncreated speech of Allah, or do you sometimes treat it like a man-made book? What would change if you internalized its divine origin more deeply?
?
Al-Bāqillānī defended the Quran's eternity using logical and linguistic arguments. How does your own conviction in this truth affect your daily relationship with the Quran—does it make you more reverent, or have you become complacent?

Interpreting 'Muhdath Dhikr': Baqillani's Defense of the Uncreated Quran
Baqillani explains the meaning of 'muhdath dhikr' in the verse to defend the uncreated Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the claim that the verse 'There does not come to them a reminder from their Lord that is newly renewed' proves the Quran is created.

Core Idea
The verse describes prophetic reminders as created, not the Quran itself.

Baqillani faces an objection from those who claim the Quranic verse 'ما يأتيهم من ذكر من ربهم محدث' indicates the Quran is created. In this passage, he carefully reinterprets 'dhikr' to mean the Prophet's preaching and warnings, not the Quran. He then uses logical argumentation to show the verse actually supports the Ash'ari doctrine of the Quran's uncreatedness. This lesson breaks down his key moves.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the opening question: 'فإن قالوا: فما معنى قوله: ما يأتيهم من ذكر من ربهم محدث؟' This is the objection that the verse implies the Quran is created.
The objectors use the word 'muhdath' (newly renewed) to argue that the Quran is created.
2
Notice how Baqillani redefines 'dhikr'
Baqillani responds by interpreting 'dhikr' as prophetic preaching, warning, and threat (وعظ, وعد, تخويف), not the Quran. He supports this with other Quranic verses like 'فذكر إنما أنت مذكر' and the phrase 'مجلس الذكر'.
He also suggests an alternative interpretation: the verse refers to prophets themselves, as in 'ذكرا رسولا'.
3
Follow his logical argument about redundancy
Baqillani argues that if all 'dhikr' were 'muhdath', the description would be redundant, like saying 'a male man'. Thus, describing 'dhikr' as 'muhdath' implies some 'dhikr' is not 'muhdath', which must be the Quran.
This is a key kalam argument: description implies distinction.
4
Understand his conclusion
Baqillani concludes that the verse actually supports the view that the Quran is uncreated (not muhdath), because everything else called 'dhikr' (prophetic teachings) is created by consensus.
He states: 'فوجب أن يكون نعت الذكر بالحدوث دلالة على أنه منه ما ليس بمحدث... وهو القرآن'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: فما معنى قوله: ما يأتيهم من ذكر من ربهم محدث؟ قيل لهم: معناه ما يأتيهم من وعظ من النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ووعد وتخويف إلا استمعوه وهم يلعبون. لأن وعظ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ووعيده وتحذيره ذكر. قال الله تبارك وتعالى: فذكر إنما أنت مذكر. ويقال: فلان في مجلس الذكر. وهذا أولى لأن قريشاً لم تضحك وتلعب بالقرآن ولكن أفحمت عند سماعه وتشتت فيه أهواؤهم وآراؤهم. ويحتمل أن يكون أراد: ما يأتيهم من نبي بعد نبي إلا استمعوا قوله ولعبوا وأعرضوا عنه. وقد سمى الله تعالى الرسول ذكراً فقال: ذكرا رسولا يتلو عليكم آيات الله. وأيضاً فإن الله تعالى لم يقل: ما يأتيهم من ذكر من ربهم إلا كان محدثاً. وفي الآية دلالة على أن في الذكر ما ليس بمحدث لأجل نعته للذكر بالحدوث. ولو كان لا ذكر إلا محدث لم يكن لقوله: من ذكر من ربهم محدث معنى. كما أنه لا معنى لقول القائل: ما يأتيني من رجل ذكر إلا أكرمته ولا هاشمي شريف إلا قدمته إذا كان الرجل لا يكون إلا ذكراً والهاشمي لا يكون إلا شريفاً. فوجب أن يكون نعت الذكر بالحدوث دلالة على أنه منه ما ليس بمحدث فيجب أن يكون هو القرآن للإجماع على أن كل ما عداه من الذكر محدث. واختلافنا في كلام الله سبحانه والآية بأن تدل على قولنا أقرب.
For they said: What is the meaning of His statement: 'There does not come to them a reminder from their Lord that is newly renewed'? It was said to them: Its meaning is that there does not come to them any preaching from the Prophet, peace be upon him, and promise and threat except they listen to it while they play. Because the preaching of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and his warning and his cautioning are a reminder. Allah, blessed and exalted, said: 'So remind, for you are only a reminder.' And it is said: 'So-and-so is in a gathering of remembrance.' And this is more appropriate because Quraysh did not laugh and play with the Quran, but they were silenced at hearing it and their desires and opinions were scattered. And it is possible that He meant: There does not come to them a prophet after a prophet except they listen to his statement and play and turn away from it. And Allah, exalted, has called the messenger a reminder, saying: 'A reminder, a messenger reciting to you the verses of Allah.' Also, Allah, exalted, did not say: 'There does not come to them a reminder from their Lord except it is newly renewed.' And in the verse there is an indication that among the reminder there is what is not newly renewed because of His description of the reminder with newness. And if there were no reminder except newly renewed, then His statement: 'from a reminder from their Lord newly renewed' would have no meaning. Just as there is no meaning to the statement of one who says: 'There does not come to me a male man except I honor him, nor a noble Hashimi except I advance him,' if a man is only male and the Hashimi is only noble. So it is necessary that the description of the reminder with newness is an indication that among it there is what is not newly renewed, so it must be the Quran, by consensus that everything else of the reminder is newly renewed. And our disagreement is concerning the speech of Allah, the Glorious, and the verse is closer to indicating our statement.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (مسألة) typical of kalam argumentation. The objectors cite the Quranic verse 'ما يأتيهم من ذكر من ربهم محدث' (There does not come to them a reminder from their Lord that is newly renewed) to argue that the Quran is created (muhdath). Baqillani responds by interpreting 'dhikr' (reminder) as prophetic preaching, warning, and threat—not the Quran itself. He supports this by noting that Quraysh mocked prophetic admonitions but were awed by the Quran. He also argues that the verse itself distinguishes between 'dhikr' and what is 'muhdath' (newly renewed) by describing the reminder as muhdath; if all reminder were muhdath, the description would be redundant. This is like saying 'a male man' when all men are male. Thus, the description indicates that some reminder is not muhdath, and that must be the Quran, since all other reminder (prophetic teachings) are created. He concludes that the verse supports the Ash'ari position that the Quran is uncreated.

ذكر
محدث
قرآن
كلام الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'There does not come to them a reminder from their Lord that is newly renewed' means the Quran is created.
Correction: Baqillani shows that 'dhikr' refers to prophetic preaching, not the Quran, and the description of 'muhdath' actually indicates some reminder is not muhdath, which is the Quran.

Checkpoint

A) The Quran itself
B) Prophetic preaching, warnings, and threats
C) All forms of divine speech
D) The Torah and Gospel

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the claim that the Quran is created by reinterpreting 'muhdath dhikr' as prophetic reminders, not the Quran itself.

The Meaning of 'Amr Allah' in the Quran
Baqillani refutes the claim that 'amr Allah' refers to His speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani distinguishes between 'amr' as speech and 'amr' as action/decree in Quranic verses.

Core Idea
'Amr Allah' often means His actions and decrees, not merely His speech.

This lesson tackles a common objection raised by those who claim that God's command (amr) is created because it appears in verses as an accomplished matter. Baqillani responds by explaining that in such contexts, 'amr' refers to punishment, victory, or decreed actions, not the speech command. Let's read his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objectors cite verses like 'And the command of Allah is an accomplished matter' to argue that 'amr' is an act of God, implying it is created.
The verses are 'وكان أمر الله مفعولاً' and 'قدرا مقدوراً'.
2
Understand Baqillani's response
Baqillani interprets 'amr' here as punishment and victory, not speech. He says it refers to His punishment, vengeance, victory for believers, and decreed actions.
This shifts the meaning from speech to action.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
He gives two more Quranic examples: 'Our command came' (punishment) and 'the command of Pharaoh was not right' (his affairs). These show 'amr' meaning action or situation.
Context determines meaning.
4
Review the poetic evidence
Two lines of Arabic poetry are cited where 'amr' means affairs or actions, confirming the linguistic usage.
The poets use 'amr' for personal matters.
5
Distinguish the plural forms
Baqillani notes that the plural of 'amr' (command/speech) is 'awamir', while the plural of 'amr' (affair) is 'umur'. The objectors confuse them.
This linguistic point refutes their claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: فما معنى قوله تعالى: وكان أمر الله مفعولاً وقدرا مقدوراً؟ قيل لهم: أراد تعالى عقابه وانتقامه من الكافرين ونصره للمؤمنين ومن حكم به وقدره من الأفعال. ومن ذلك قوله تعالى: جاء أمرنا، وقوله: وما أمر فرعون برشيد يعني شأنه وأفعاله وطرائقه. قال الشاعر: لها أمرها حتى إذا ما تبوأت بأخفافها مرعى تبراً مضجعاً. وقال آخر: فقلت لها أمري إلى الله كله وإني إليه في الإياب لراجع يعني شؤوني وأفعالي ولم يرد بذلك الأمر الذي هو القول. هذا أمور وجمع الأمر من القول أوامر. ولولا العجز لم يلجأوا إلى مثل هذا التمويه.
For they said: What is the meaning of His statement, exalted: 'And the command of Allah is an accomplished matter' and 'a determined decree'? It was said to them: He, exalted, meant His punishment and His vengeance against the disbelievers, and His victory for the believers, and what He decreed and determined of actions. And from that is His statement: 'Our command came' and His statement: 'And the command of Pharaoh was not right' meaning his affair and his actions and his ways. The poet said: 'She has her command until when she settled with her hooves a pasture of dust, a resting place.' And another said: 'I said to her: My command is all to Allah, and I to Him in return am returning' meaning my affairs and my actions, and He did not intend by that the command which is speech. This (word) is 'umur' (affairs), and the plural of 'amr' (command) from speech is 'awamir'. And were it not for inability, they would not resort to such obfuscation.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is another objection-and-answer (مسألة). The objectors cite verses like 'وكان أمر الله مفعولاً' (And the command of Allah is an accomplished matter) and 'قدرا مقدوراً' (a determined decree) to argue that 'amr' (command) is an act of God, implying it is created. Baqillani responds that 'amr' here does not mean speech but rather punishment, vengeance, victory, and decreed actions. He supports this with Quranic examples: 'جاء أمرنا' (Our command came) means the punishment, and 'ما أمر فرعون برشيد' (the command of Pharaoh was not right) means his affairs and actions. He also cites Arabic poetry where 'amr' means affairs or actions, not speech. He distinguishes the plural forms: 'أمور' (umur) for affairs, and 'أوامر' (awamir) for commands (speech). He concludes that the objectors' claim is a weak obfuscation they resort to out of inability.

Key Terms
أمر
مفعول
قدر
أمور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'amr Allah' in the Quran always and only refers to God's speech (the eternal attribute of speech).
Correction: Baqillani demonstrates that 'amr' often refers to actions, decrees, punishments, and affairs, as shown by context and Arabic usage.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani say 'amr Allah' means in the verse 'وكان أمر الله مفعولاً'?
A) God's command as speech
B) God's punishment and vengeance
C) God's decree only
D) God's creation

Key Takeaway
Baqillani demonstrates that 'amr' in the Quran often refers to God's actions and decrees, not His speech, refuting the claim that it is created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses like 'wa kāna amru llāhi maf'ūlā', do you habitually assume 'amr' means 'speech'? After this lesson, will you now pause and consider the context before assigning meaning? Commit to studying ambiguous Quranic terms with scholarly tools.
?
Baqillani calls his opponents' argument 'obfuscation due to inability'. Are there areas in your own faith where you stubbornly cling to a comfortable interpretation because you lack the courage to dig deeper? Identify one such belief and resolve to investigate it with sound methodology.
?
This lesson shows how a single word can shift meaning across the Quran. Have you ever built a theological conclusion on a misunderstood term? Today, ask yourself: 'Am I willing to let the Quran correct my assumptions, even if it unsettles me?'

Mu'tazila on createdness of God's word
Baqillani refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that God's word is created and like human speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite position on the createdness of God's word and Baqillani's refutation linking it to Christian doctrine.

Core Idea
God's speech is eternal, not created like human speech.

Baqillani begins his refutation by quoting the Mu'tazilite view directly. He shows that they follow the Christians in claiming that God's word is created and dwells in a body. This lesson introduces that claim and prepares us to understand the proper Sunni position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to familiarize yourself with the Mu'tazilite position.
2
Identify the two claims
Note that the Mu'tazila make two assertions: God's word is created, and it is similar to human speech.
Baqillani links them to Christian doctrine.
3
Understand the refutation
Consider why Baqillani presents this as a refutation. The implied contrast is that God's speech is eternal and unlike creation.
This connection to Christianity is meant to discredit the view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت المعتزلة بأسرها إن كلمة الله تعالى مخلوقة، يخلقها في الشجرة وغيرها من الأجسام اتباعاً منهم للنصارى في قولهم إن كلمة الله مخلوقة من وجه وحالة في جسد مخلوق. وقالوا بأسرهم إن كلام الله تعالى من جنس كلام البشر ومثل له.
And the Mu'tazila all together said that the word of Allah is created, He creates it in the tree and other bodies, following the Christians in their saying that the word of Allah is created in a certain respect and dwelling in a created body. And they all together said that the speech of Allah is of the same kind as human speech and similar to it.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage records the Mu'tazilite position on the divine word. They assert that God's word (كلمة) is created (مخلوقة) and that God creates it in physical objects like the tree from which Moses heard it. Baqillani notes that they follow Christian doctrine, which holds that God's word was created and dwelt in a body (Jesus). Additionally, they equate God's speech with human speech, claiming it is of the same kind and similar. This is a refutation: Baqillani implies that God's speech is eternal and unlike creation.

Correction: The Mu'tazila believe God's word is created and similar to human speech, which contradicts the Sunni view that it is an eternal attribute.

The Mu'tazila claim that God's word is created and similar to human speech is refuted by Baqillani as imitating Christian doctrine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Question of Seeing God
Baqillani poses the opening question about seeing God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opening question in Baqillani's discussion on the permissibility of seeing God with physical eyes.

Core Idea
The debate on seeing God begins with a question of possibility.

Baqillani opens this section by posing a fundamental question: Can the Eternal God be seen with physical eyes? This question sets the stage for a detailed theological argument. However, due to a textual gap, the answer is not fully preserved in this passage, and we only have the question and a brief cue that an answer follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic heading and question once. Notice how the author frames the issue as a hypothetical objection from an interlocutor.
The phrase 'قيل' indicates that an answer is coming, but it is missing here.
2
Identify the core question
The question asks about the permissibility (جواز) of seeing the Eternal (القديم) with eyes (الأبصار). Underline these key terms.
3
Reflect on the missing answer
Consider why this question is important for kalam. How might a theologian defend the possibility of seeing God? The missing answer in this source reminds us to consult other pages for Baqillani's full argument.

Refuting Poetic Objections to Seeing God
Baqillani interprets a line of poetry to refute an objection against seeing God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani interprets a poetic use of 'nāẓir' to defend the doctrine that God cannot be seen in this world.

Core Idea
A hopeful look is not the same as literal vision.

This lesson examines a specific objection raised against the Ash'ari position that God cannot be seen with physical eyes in this life. The objectors cite a line of poetry by Jamil bin Ma'mar, where the poet says he is 'looking' (nāẓir) toward God. Does this prove that literal vision of God is possible? Baqillani offers a careful interpretation of the verse, showing that the word carries a different meaning in context.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the poetic line
Start by reading the objectors' citation of Jamil's poetry. Notice how they use the word 'nāẓir' to argue for the possibility of seeing God.
The line is: 'Indeed I am toward You, because of what You promised, looking (nāẓir) / the look of the lowly one to the Mighty, the All-Powerful.'
2
Analyze Baqillani's interpretation
Baqillani explains that 'nāẓir' here means 'look of the eye' but accompanied by abasement and humility, not literal vision of God's essence. The poet is looking in hope and expectation.
Key terms: نظر العين (look of the eye), الذل (abasement), الانكسار (humility), مقتضياً (demanding), متنجزاً (expecting).
3
Connect to the larger argument
Understand that this refutation defends the Ash'ari position that God is not seen with physical eyes in this world, and that poetic evidence must be interpreted in light of linguistic context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : فما معنى قول جميل بن معمر: إنِّي إِلَيْكَ لِمَا وَعَدْتَ لَنَاظِرُ نَظَرَ الدُّلِيلِ إِلَى الْعَزِيزِ الْقَاهِرِ قيل لهم : معناه نظر العين المقرون بالذل والانكسار، لأنه نظر إليه ببصره مقتضياً متنجزاً لوعده نظر الذليل إلى العزيز القاهر.
For they said: What is the meaning of the saying of Jamil bin Ma'mar: 'Indeed I am toward You, because of what You promised, looking (nāẓir) / the look of the lowly one to the Mighty, the All-Powerful'? It was said to them: Its meaning is the look of the eye accompanied by abasement and humility, because he looked at Him with his eyesight, demanding and expecting His promise, like the look of the lowly one to the Mighty, the All-Powerful.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objectors cite a line of poetry by Jamil bin Ma'mar where he says 'I am looking toward You' in the context of awaiting a promise. They argue that the word 'nāẓir' here means literal seeing with the eyes, and since the poet uses it for God, it would support the possibility of seeing God. The author (Baqillani) responds by explaining that 'nāẓir' in this verse does not mean literal vision of God, but rather the look of the eye that is accompanied by abasement and humility. The poet is describing himself as looking towards God with his eyesight, but not seeing God's essence; rather, he is looking in hope and expectation of receiving His promise. The phrase 'نظر الدليل إلى العزيز القاهر' (the look of the lowly one to the Mighty, the All-Powerful) indicates that this is a look of supplication and humility, not of literal sight. Thus, the poetic evidence does not support the objectors' claim.

Key Terms
ناظر
نظر العين
الذل
الانكسار
مقتضياً
متنجزاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'nāẓir' in Arabic poetry always implies literal physical vision.
Correction: Baqillani demonstrates that 'nāẓir' can indicate a look of hope, expectation, and humility, not necessarily direct sight.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani say is the meaning of 'nāẓir' in the line of Jamil bin Ma'mar?
A) Literal vision of God
B) A look of the eye accompanied by humility and expectation
C) A metaphorical look of the heart
D) A look of anger

Key Takeaway
Baqillani shows that linguistic context determines meaning: a poetic 'look' at God can express hope and humility, not literal sight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself interpreting Quranic verses or hadith about 'seeing Allah' (like 'وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة') with a literal mindset, forgetting that the word 'nāẓir' can carry a meaning of hopeful expectation and humility? How will you train yourself to consider linguistic context before forming a theological conclusion?
?
When you supplicate to Allah, do you look toward Him with the same abasement and brokenness described in this lesson? Or have you become accustomed to a casual, demanding attitude in your prayers? What specific change will you make today to cultivate that humility?
?
The poet Jamil bin Ma'mar expressed his reliance on Allah's promise with a physical look upward. How often do you physically or mentally 'look' toward Allah with expectant hope rather than despair? What promise from Allah are you waiting for right now that requires you to adopt this posture of humble expectation?

Refutation of Poetic Objection from Hassan bin Thabit
Baqillani interprets Hassan's line about looking to the Merciful.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani interprets a poetic verse that might imply seeing God, and see that it refers to seeking victory from the sky, not literal vision.

Core Idea
Poetic language about 'seeing God' points to hope, not physical sight.

Objectors sometimes quote poetry to suggest that early Muslims believed in seeing God. Baqillani responds by examining a line from the Companion poet Hassan bin Thabit. He shows that the words really refer to looking towards the sky for divine help, not to literal vision of Allah. This helps preserve divine transcendence while honoring the Companions' faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and verse
First, read the objection as presented by Baqillani: the opponents quote Hassan's line about 'faces looking (nāẓirāt) to the Merciful'. Understand why this seems to challenge the doctrine that God is not seen in this world.
The verse is from the Battle of Badr.
2
Examine Baqillani's interpretive response
Baqillani explains that 'looking' here means the physical act of looking with the eyes toward the sky, not seeing God's essence. The Companions were looking to the sky as the direction from which they hoped for victory.
Nāẓirāt can mean 'waiting' or 'looking', but Baqillani chooses 'looking with the eyes'.
3
Understand the phrase 'to the Merciful'
Baqillani says the phrase 'to the Merciful' means 'toward the sky of the Merciful' and 'the direction of desire toward Him'. It does not mean waiting (al-intiẓār) but hoping for help.
This interpretation safeguards tanzih (divine transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : فما معنى قول حسان بن ثابت: وُجُوهٌ نَاظِرَاتٌ يَوْمَ بَدْرٍ إِلَى الرَّحْمَنِ يَأْتِي بِالْفَلَاح قيل لهم : أراد نظر الأبصار إلى سماء الرحمن وترقب النصر عند رميهم بالأبصار إلى الجهة التي منها يرجى النصر؛ وقوله إلى الرحمن يعني به إلى سماء الرحمن وجهة الرغبة إليه، ولم يرد الانتظار.
For they said: What is the meaning of the saying of Hassan bin Thabit: 'Faces looking (nāẓirāt) on the day of Badr to the Merciful, He brings success'? It was said to them: He intended the seeing of the eyes toward the sky of the Merciful and awaiting victory when they cast their eyes toward the direction from which victory is hoped; and his saying 'to the Merciful' means by it toward the sky of the Merciful and the direction of desire toward Him, and he did not intend waiting (al-intiẓār).
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This is another objection-and-answer. The objectors cite a line from Hassan bin Thabit that speaks of 'faces looking (nāẓirāt) on the day of Badr to the Merciful'. They imply that this shows the Companions were looking at God. The author responds by explaining that 'nāẓirāt' here means the looking of the eyes (نظر الأبصار) toward the sky where God's mercy descends, and that they were looking for victory from that direction. The phrase 'إلى الرحمن' (to the Merciful) is interpreted as 'toward the sky of the Merciful' and 'the direction of desire toward Him'. The author specifically says that Hassan did not mean waiting (الانتظار), but rather hoping for help. Thus, the verse does not imply literal vision of God.

Key Terms
ناظرات
نظر الأبصار
سماء الرحمن
ترقب النصر
جهة الرغبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Hassan's line 'faces looking to the Merciful' means the Companions literally saw Allah with their eyes.
Correction: Baqillani clarifies that 'looking' (nāẓirāt) here means physical looking toward the sky for victory, and 'to the Merciful' refers to the sky of the Merciful and direction of desire, not literal sight of God.

Checkpoint
How does Baqillani interpret Hassan's line 'faces looking to the Merciful'?
A) They were seeing God with their eyes
B) They were looking towards the sky hoping for victory
C) They were waiting for a sign
D) They were contemplating God's essence

Key Takeaway
Hassan's verse refers to the Companions looking to the sky for God's help, not to seeing Allah directly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Companions on the day of Badr looked up to the sky, trusting that victory comes only from Allah. In your own trials, do you physically or mentally 'look up' to Allah with that same hope, or do you rely on other means first? What would change if you truly directed every need to Him as they did?
?
Hassan's poetic phrase was misunderstood by some as claiming direct sight of Allah. Are there any verses or phrases in your own understanding that you might be taking too literally without considering context? How can you train yourself to seek correct interpretation before forming beliefs?
?
Baqillani explained that the Companions were looking to the sky as a direction of prayer and hope, not seeing Allah. When you recite or hear phrases like 'to the Merciful,' does your heart immediately turn to Him in need, or do you let the words pass without engaging your soul? What practical step can you take today to make every mention of Allah a call to direct your hope to Him?

Refutation of Allegorical Interpretation of Quranic Verse
Baqillani rejects metaphorical interpretation without proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani insists that the literal meaning of the Quranic verse 'looking to their Lord' is the only valid interpretation without a proof for metaphor.

Core Idea
Without proof, no Quranic verse can be interpreted metaphorically.

In this passage, Baqillani confronts those who propose an alternative interpretation of the verse describing the believers looking to their Lord. The objectors suggest it means looking at gardens and acts of God, not God Himself. Baqillani responds with a key principle: the literal meaning holds unless there is evidence to justify a metaphorical reading.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that this is a short objection-and-answer exchange.
2
Identify the objection and response
The objectors propose that the verse means looking at gardens and deeds, not God. Baqillani responds by stating that this makes it metaphorical, which is impermissible without proof.
Contrast the literal reading with the metaphorical one.
3
Understand the rule
Why can't we adopt a metaphorical interpretation without proof? This is because the literal meaning is the default, and any deviation requires justification.
This principle applies to all Quranic interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : فما أنكرتم أيضاً أن يكون الله تعالى إنما أراد بقوله: إلى ربها ناظرة، أي: إنها إلى جنان ربّها وأفعاله وعظيم ما أعدّه لأولئك ناظرة؟ قيل لهم : هذا التأويل يجعل الآية مجازاً، وليس لنا فعل ذلك إلا بحجة.
For they said: What prevents you also from [saying] that Allah Almighty only meant by His saying 'to their Lord looking' (إلى ربها ناظرة) i.e., that they are looking toward the gardens of their Lord and His actions and the great things He has prepared for them? It was said to them: This interpretation makes the verse metaphorical, and it is not permissible for us to do that except with proof.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the Quranic verse 'إلى ربها ناظرة' (To their Lord looking) from Surah Al-Qiyamah. The objectors propose an alternative interpretation: that 'looking' here means looking at the gardens and actions of God, rather than seeing God Himself. The author (Baqillani) responds by stating that such an interpretation makes the verse metaphorical (مجاز), and it is not permissible to adopt a metaphorical interpretation without proof (حجة). Since the literal meaning (seeing God) is possible and there is no proof to divert from it, the objection is invalid. The author thus upholds the literal understanding that the believers will see God in the afterlife.

Key Terms
إلى ربها ناظرة
مجاز
حجة
تأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse can be interpreted metaphorically without any evidence.
Correction: Baqillani states that metaphorical interpretation is only permissible when there is a valid proof (حجة). Without proof, the literal meaning must be retained.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani reject the interpretation that 'to their Lord looking' means looking at gardens and deeds?
A) Because it is grammatically incorrect
B) Because it makes the verse metaphorical without proof
C) Because gardens are not mentioned in the verse
D) Because it contradicts the consensus

Key Takeaway
Literal interpretation is the default; metaphorical interpretation requires proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'إلى ربها ناظرة', does your heart yearn to see Allah? Or have you allowed intellectual doubts to weaken that hope? How can you strengthen your certainty in this literal promise starting today?
?
Baqillani says we cannot interpret metaphorically without proof. Examine your own faith: are there any beliefs or verses you have reinterpreted to fit your comfort rather than accepting the apparent meaning? Will you commit to seeking proof before departing from the clear text?
?
This lesson demands that you treat Allah's words with utmost seriousness. Have you ever accepted a metaphorical interpretation of a Quranic verse without evidence because it felt more reasonable? What does your response reveal about your reverence for divine revelation?

Refutation of Poetic Objection: 'Looking at Death'
Baqillani explains that 'looking at death' is metaphorical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the poetic objection by explaining that 'looking at death' is metaphorical, referring to the physical causes or heroes of death.

Core Idea
Poetic 'looking at death' is a metaphor for physical causes, not literal.

This passage addresses an objection based on a poetic line that speaks of 'faces looking at death'. The objectors argue that if 'looking' can be directed at death (which is invisible), then perhaps sight of God is not literal. Baqillani responds by clarifying the metaphorical usage, showing that the poet means the causes of death — striking and stabbing. He even offers a second interpretation referencing heroic figures.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully: the poet says faces are 'looking at death', but death cannot be seen. Why would this be a problem for the Ash'ari view?
The objectors claim that if 'looking' can be used for something invisible, it could be used for God without literal sight.
2
Explore the first metaphorical interpretation
Baqillani explains that 'death' here means the causes of death: striking, stabbing, and splitting skulls. In Arabic, the cause is often called by the name of the effect.
Thus, 'looking at death' means looking at the physical actions that lead to death.
3
Consider the second interpretation
Baqillani offers an alternative: the poet may have meant the heroes who bring death. He cites Jarir's line 'I am death' as a parallel.
This shows that 'death' can be used figuratively for people or objects associated with it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : فما معنى قول الشاعر : وَيَوْمَ بِذِي قَارٍ رَأيْتُ وُجُوهَهُمْ إلَى الْمَوْتِ مِنْ وَقْعِ السُّيُوفِ نَوَاظِرُ والموت لا يُرى ولا يُنظر إليه؟ قيل لهم : ما أراد الشاعر إلا رؤية الأبصار ؛ وإنما أراد بالموت الضرب والطعن وفلق الهام، لأن ذلك يُسمى في اللغة موتاً، لأنه أسباب الموت وما يقع عنده غالباً والشيء عندهم يُسمّى باسم سببه. ويمكن أيضاً أن يكون أراد بذكر الموت الأبطال الذين يوجد الموت عند كرّها وإقدامها. قال جرير: أنا المَوْتُ الَّذِي حُبِّرتَ عَنْهُ فَلَيْسَ لِهَارِبِ مِنِّي نَجَاءُ.
For they said: What is the meaning of the poet's saying: 'And on the day of Dhi Qar, I saw their faces toward death from the strike of swords looking (nawāẓir)' while death cannot be seen nor looked at? It was said to them: The poet intended only the seeing of the eyes; he intended by 'death' the striking and stabbing and splitting of skulls, because that is called 'death' in the language, since they are the causes of death and what mostly occurs at it, and a thing is called by the name of its cause. It is also possible that he intended by mentioning death the heroes at whose charge and advance death occurs. Jarir said: 'I am death about which you have been informed, so there is no escape for any fugitive from me.'
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This is the final objection-and-answer. The objectors cite a line of poetry that says 'faces toward death looking (nawāẓir)', but death is not a visible object. They argue that if 'looking' can be used for something invisible like death, then it could be used for God without implying literal sight. The author responds by explaining that the poet does not mean literal looking at death; rather, the word 'death' is used metaphorically for the causes of death: striking, stabbing, and splitting skulls. In Arabic, the cause is often called by the name of its effect. So 'looking at death' means looking at the violent actions that lead to death. The author also offers another interpretation: the poet may have meant the heroes who bring death, as when Jarir says 'I am death'. Thus, the poetic usage does not imply that seeing God is impossible; rather, it reinforces that 'looking' can be directed toward physical entities like the sky or the causes of death, but not toward a non-physical being like God in a literal sense. This supports the Ash'ari position that God can be seen but not by literal eyesight in this world.

Key Terms
نواظر
الموت
الضرب
الطعن
فلق الهام

جرير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if 'looking' can be used for non-physical death, it could be used for God without literal sight.
Correction: Baqillani clarifies that the poetic usage is metaphorical: 'death' refers to physical causes or heroes, not the abstract concept of death. Thus, 'looking' is still directed at physical entities.

Checkpoint
How does Baqillani explain the phrase 'faces looking at death' in the poem?
A) The faces were physically seeing death
B) They were looking at the causes of death like swords
C) They were looking at the concept of death
D) The poem is not authentic

Key Takeaway
Poetic references to 'looking at death' are metaphorical, referring to physical causes or heroes, and do not undermine the doctrine that God can be seen in a literal sense.
